2.3.11 Continued Consultation and Adaptive Management (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
SERCOVA  Project  has  developed  a  comprehensive  plan  to  ensure  ongoing  communication  and 
consultation  between  the  project  proponents  and  communities,  as  well  as  other  stakeholders 
throughout the project's life cycle. This plan emphasizes the importance of maintaining an open 
dialogue and fostering trust, allowing for continuous feedback from all parties involved. Regular 
meetings,  workshops,  and  forums  will  be  organized  to  provide  stakeholders  with  updates  on 
project developments, share outcomes, and discuss any concerns or suggestions that may arise. 
 
To effectively incorporate stakeholder input into project management, the SERCOVA project will 
establish a structured feedback mechanism. This mechanism will include methods such as focus 
groups,  and  community  advisory  boards,  enabling  stakeholders  to  share  their  thoughts  and 
experiences  regarding  the  project's  implementation.  The  project  team  will  actively  analyze  this 
feedback   and   integrate   it   into   decision-making   processes,   ensuring   that   any   necessary 
adjustments to management practices are made in response to community needs and concerns. 
 
Adaptive management will be a core principle of the SERCOVA project, allowing for flexibility and 
responsiveness  throughout  its  implementation.  Regular  assessments  of  project  progress  and 
stakeholder feedback will guide adjustments to strategies and practices as required. By fostering 
a  culture  of  learning  and  adaptation,  the  project  will  be  better  equipped  to  address  emerging 
challenges and opportunities, ultimately enhancing its effectiveness and ensuring alignment with 
the expectations and aspirations of the communities involved. 
 
2.3.12 Stakeholder Consultation Channels (CCB, G3.5) 
The SERCOVA project will ensure that all consultation and participatory processes are conducted 
directly  with  communities  and  other  stakeholders  or  through  their  legitimate  representatives.  To 
achieve this, the project will identify and engage community leaders, civil society organizations, and 
relevant  local  groups  at  every  stage  of  the  consultation.  This  approach  will  help  ensure  that  the 
voices and concerns of the community are heard and considered in the decision-making process. 
During the consultation process, the project will use various communication channels appropriate 
for reaching all stakeholders. This includes face-to-face meetings, discussion forums, and surveys, 
allowing  active  participation  from  all  community  groups.  This  way,  information  can  be  conveyed 
clearly and transparently, and feedback from the community can be collected directly. It will also 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
51 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
help  identify  issues  or  challenges  the  community  may  face,  enabling  the  project  to  respond 
appropriately. 
 
Justification for adequate levels of information sharing will be based on documentation and records 
of all consultation activities. The project will maintain detailed records of meetings held, including 
dates, locations, participants, and discussion outcomes. Furthermore, relevant information about 
the  project’s  objectives,  benefits,  and  impacts  will  be  provided  to  the  community  in  easily 
understandable language and accessible formats. Through this approach, the project ensures that 
all involved parties have a clear understanding of the project, and the decisions made reflect the 
interests and needs of the community. 
 
2.3.13 Stakeholder Participation in Decision-Making and Implementation (VCS, 3.18, 
3.19; CCB, G3.6) 
SERCOVA project  will  implement  various  measures  to  ensure  the  effective  participation  of  all 
communities in decision-making and project implementation. This participation will involve all local 
stakeholders,    including    indigenous    communities,    women,    youth,    and    other    potentially 
underrepresented groups. The project will create open dialogue spaces where all parties can voice 
their opinions and aspirations, ensuring that every group’s voice is valued at every stage of the 
project. 
 
These measures will be designed with cultural and gender sensitivity in mind. The project will take 
an  approach  that  respects  local  values  and  norms,  ensuring  that  community  involvement  is  not 
only  appropriate  but  also  respectful  of  local  wisdom.  Special  attention  will  be  given  to  women’s 
participation,   ensuring   they   have   equal   access   to   decision-making   processes   and   field 
implementation activities. 
 
To  maintain  this  sensitivity,  training  and  orientation  will  be  adapted  to  the  cultural  and  gender 
backgrounds of each group. The project team will be trained to understand the local context and 
ensure  inclusivity  in  every  aspect  of  interaction  with  the  community.  This  way,  community 
participation in the project will not only be effective but will also build trust and support for the long-
term success of the project. 
 
2.3.14 Anti-Discrimination Assurance (VCS 3.19; CCB, G3.7) 
SERCOVA Project holds every project personnel (hired or received benefit from the project) to a 
standard of integrity to ensure that all parties involved in SERCOVA project do not engage in or 
support any form of discrimination. This include discrimination based on gender, race, religion, 
or sexual orientation according to all relevant Indonesia labor laws and regulations such as Law 
No. 39 of 1999 on Human Rights, Law No. 13 of 2003 on Employment, Minister of Manpower 
and Transmigration Regulation No. 13 of 2011. 
Clear anti-discrimination policies will be implemented and followed by all employees and project 
partners. Specific training on equal rights and obligations will be provided to ensure an inclusive 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
52 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
and discrimination-free working environment. In addition, the project will implement monitoring 
measures  to  ensure  that  no  sexual  harassment  occurs  during  project  activities.  Harassment 
prevention  policies  will  be  strengthened  through  training  and  regular  oversight,  along  with 
providing a safe reporting mechanism for all parties. 
 
In addition, the project has a grievance system in place that will provide all project employees, 
identified community  members, and other  stakeholders with a formal  system  in  the  event that 
any  discriminatory  actions  or  sexual  harassment  does  occur,  to  ensure grievances  can  be 
addressed quickly and appropriately. To date, no staff have been sanctioned for infringement of 
these regulations, and no such grievances have been reported. 
 
2.3.15 Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.18.4; CCB, G3.8) 
SERCOVA project  is  currently  in  the  process  of formalizing the  SERCOVA  FGRM  (Feedback  and 
Grievance   Redress   Mechanism) for   any   potential   grievances   arising   from project   zone 
communities or other stakeholders. FGRM is being developed in a participatory manner to ensure 
community engagement and know-how to use and implement the FGRM mechanism. The draft 
procedures of the FGRM can be seen in the following table and diagram.  
 
Table 2-14. SERCOVA FGRM development and redressing procedure. 
Development 
process 
1. Assessment and Planning 
• Stakeholder Identification: Identify all relevant stakeholders, 
including affected communities, project staff, and local authorities.  
• Needs Assessment: The types of grievances that may arise and the 
existing mechanisms to address some of the grievance were 
considered. 
• Cultural Context: Consider local customs, traditions, and culturally 
appropriate conflict resolution methods. 
2. Designing the Grievance Redress Mechanism 
• Clear Procedures: SERCOVA project provided multiple channels 
such as in-person, text, and written submissions through letter or 
email. 
• Grievance Log: System for logging and tracking grievances to 
ensure transparency and accountability. 
• Roles and Responsibilities: Roles and responsibilities of all parties 
involved in the grievance process are identified especially SERCOVA 
staffs. 
3. Receiving Grievances 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
53 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Accessibility: FGRM is being consulted to ensure ease access to all 
stakeholders, including vulnerable groups. 
• Awareness Campaigns: Awareness campaigns to inform 
stakeholders about the grievance mechanism and how to use it is 
planned to be conducted after the FGRM finalized. 
4. Hearing and Reviewing Grievances 
• Initial Screening: Screen grievances to determine their validity and 
categorize them based on their nature and urgency. 
• Grievance Committee: Form a grievance redress committee 
comprising representatives from the project, community, and 
possibly independent experts. 
• Culturally Appropriate Methods: Use culturally appropriate methods 
for hearing grievances, such as community meetings or mediation 
sessions. 
Grievance 
redress 
procedure 
5. Responding to Grievances 
• Timely Response: Establish timelines for responding to grievances. 
The World Bank typically recommends acknowledging receipt within 
a few days and providing a resolution within a reasonable period, 
often 30-60 days. 
• Communication: Communicate the response clearly to the 
complainant, including the rationale for the decision and any 
proposed actions. 
6. Resolving Grievances 
• Action Plan: Develop and implement an action plan to address the 
grievance. This may involve corrective actions, compensation, or 
other measures. 
• Follow-Up: Follow up with the complainant to ensure the grievance 
has been satisfactorily resolved. 
7. Monitoring and Evaluation 
• Regular Monitoring: Monitor the grievance redress process regularly 
to ensure it is functioning effectively. 
• Feedback Mechanism: Collect feedback from stakeholders to 
identify areas for improvement. 
• Reporting: Report on the performance of the grievance mechanism 
to stakeholders and the World Bank. 
8. Continuous Improvement 
• Review and Update: Periodically review and update the grievance 
redress mechanism based on feedback and lessons learned. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
54 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Capacity Building: Provide ongoing training and capacity building for 
staff and stakeholders involved in the grievance process. 
 
 
 
 
Figure 2-15. The diagram process of SERCOVA FGRM 
 
2.3.16 Accessibility of the Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.19; 
CCB, G3.8) 
To  ensure  the  accessibility  of  the  feedback  and  grievance  redress  procedures  can  be  accessed 
easily by the community, the project will ensure that there is a contact person who can be contacted 
easily  in  each  village.  The  contact  person  will  be  presented  together  with  the  Feedback  and 
Grievance  Redress  Procedure  at  village  meetings,  local  government  meetings  and  socialization 
through  village  notice  boards.  Grievances  and  responses  will  be  documented  and  reported  on  a 
quarterly basis by project staff and shared with the community and relevant stakeholders.  
2.3.17 Worker Training (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.9) 
Working Training for the local community in the Sercova Project will be accompanied by orientation 
and  training  programs  designed  to  build  skills  that  are  beneficial  for the  local  population.  These 
training  programs  will  focus  on  developing  relevant  abilities,  such  as  forest  patrol,  biodiversity 
monitoring,  and  sustainable  land-use  practices.  Thus,  the  training  not  only  supports  project 
implementation  but  also  provides  skills  that  the  community  can  utilize  once  the  project  is 
completed.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
55 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.3.18 Community Employment Opportunities (VCS, 3.19.13; CCB, G3.10) 
In  addition  to  Employment  Opportunities  for  community,  the  program  will  ensure  inclusive 
participation,  involving  all  community  groups,  including  women,  youth,  and  groups  that  may  be 
underrepresented. The training will incorporate practical learning methods such as field training, 
demonstrations,   and   peer-to-peer   learning   initiatives,   allowing   participants   to   gain   better 
understanding  and  direct  application  in  project  activities.  This  ensures  that  the  community  can 
actively contribute to the project's success. 
 
To ensure that the capacity built is not lost, the project will implement effective knowledge transfer 
mechanisms.  For  instance,  more  experienced  staff  can  mentor  new  hires  through  a  mentorship 
program. Additionally, by providing clear career development pathways, the project will encourage 
local  employees  to  continue  growing  and  reduce  staff  turnover.  This  approach  helps  retain  local 
skills and knowledge, ensuring the project's long-term sustainability.  
2.3.19 Occupational Safety Assessment (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.12) 
The  project owner has reviewed  the  Labor  laws  of  Republic  of Indonesia and modified them  to 
align  with  local conditions  to  ensure the health  and  safety  of  workers.  The  workers' health and 
safety  policy,  which  encompasses  health  insurance  coverage  for  workplace  accidents  and 
evacuation procedures, is provided to workers and enforced by the village committee. 
The  primary  risks  associated  with  the  project  include  fire  hazards,  vehicular  incidents,  and 
unforeseen circumstances in the forest.  
To  mitigate  these risks,  all  workers  undergo  comprehensive  training  before  commencing  their 
duties.  This  training  addresses  necessary  risk  control  measures  and  includes  the  previously 
mentioned  health  and  safety  policy.  Additionally,  all the stations and  the village  administration 
office within the project area are equipped with first aid kits containing bandages, cotton swabs, 
hand sanitizers, disinfectants, gloves, and common medications. Workers have been informed of 
the locations of these stations and trained in the use of the first aid kits.

2.4.5 Financial Health of Implementing Organization(s) (CCB, G4.3) 
The SERCOVA Project and its proponent, PT Strata Pacific, is an established organization with stable 
cash flow. The project’s proponent has been operating in Seram for a number of years on forestry 
business  and  is  neither  seeking  for  a  loan  nor  under  debts  for  SERCOVA  Project.  This  situation 
demonstrates  a  sufficient  fund  is  already  secured  for  the  project  development  and  long-term 
implementation. Documents  supporting  these investments and outlining the project’s financial 
feasibility and breakeven can be produced for the project auditor for inspection. 
PT  Strata  Pacific  and the SERCOVA  Project  are funded through  secure  investment  financing  and 
will generate revenue from the sale of verified carbon units (VCUs). These financial mechanisms 
will  guarantee  the  execution of  all  outlined  project  activities.  Financial  forecasts  and  supporting 
documentation are available to validators upon request. 
 
2.4.6 Avoidance of Corruption and Other Unethical Behavior (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G4.3) 
SERCOVA  Project holds  every  project  personnel  (hired or  received  benefit  from  the  project)  to  a 
standard of integrity to ensure that all parties involved in SERCOVA project to comply with corporate 
regulation and associated SOPs including finance and administration. Financial control within the 
project is taken very seriously. Written financial management practices, including full segregation 
of  responsibilities,  are  enshrined  in  the  deeds  of  enactment  of  the  company  and  in  supporting 
documentation agreed on behalf of the shareholders by the Board of Directors. PT Strata Pacific 
conducts routine internal audits and undergoes annual independent external audit. Full external 
audit reports are available to the verifiers on request.

2.4.7 Commercially Sensitive Information (VCS, 3.5.2 – 3.5.4; CCB Rules, 3.5.13 – 3.5.14)  
The following types of information are considered commercially sensitive and are not available 
to the public: 
• Detailed  Financial  Data:  This  involves  specific  figures  about  the  project's  costs  and 
financial  projections  over the  project  lifetime.  Such details  are  not  shared  publicly as 
revealing sensitive financial strategies and structures. 
• Proprietary   Technology   or   Methods: Information   about   proprietary   technologies, 
methodologies,  or  processes  used  in  the  project  is  not  disclosed.  These  details  are 
confidential and could provide a competitive advantage if shared. It will only be shared 
to the VVB if it is required. 
• Contracts and Agreements: All agreements between the project team and their partners, 
such as technical experts, local communities, and government bodies, are not disclosed. 
These agreements contain important terms and conditions  for  project  implementation, 
which are sensitive. 
• GIS and spatial data: All GIS and spatial data are sensitive data according to Indonesia 
regulation and policies.  
For more details, please referring to Appendix 4

2.5.2 Relevant Laws and Regulations Related to Worker’s Rights (VCS, 3.18.2; CCB, 
G3.11) 
SERCOVA Project recognizes the importance of upholding workers' rights and ensure compliance 
with all relevant laws and regulations in Indonesia. SERCOVA Project operations fully acknowledge 
the rights of workers and seek to ensure that the rights of all employees are upheld in a manner 
where all individuals are aware of their rights. Below is a list of key legislation governing workers' 
rights, along with assurances that the project meets or exceeds each requirement. 
• Law  No.  13  of  2003  on Employment – Governs  labor  rights  and  workplace  conditions, 
ensuring fair treatment of workers involved in the project. This law regulates various aspects 
of labor relations, including employment contracts, wages, working hours, and termination 
procedures 
• Government  Regulation  of  the  Republic  of  Indonesia  no. 44/2015 on  Work  Accident 
Insurance and Death Insurance Program  
• Government Regulation No. 35/2021, on Fixed-Term Employment Agreement, Outsourcing, 
Working Hours and Rest Hours, and Termination of Employment 
• Government Regulation No. 36/2021, on Wages 
• Presidential Regulation of the Republic of Indonesia no. 82/2018 on Health Insurance. 
 
By implementing these measures, the SERCOVA project aims to create a fair, safe, and equitable 
working  environment,  fostering  a  culture  of  respect  for  workers'  rights  and  contributing  to  their 
overall well-being. 
 
2.5.3 Human Rights (VCS, 3.19)  
SERCOVA project is committed to recognizing, respecting, and promoting the rights of Indigenous 
Peoples (IPs), local communities (LCs), and customary rights holders. This commitment aligns with 
applicable international human rights law, including the United Nations Declaration on the Rights 
of Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP) and the International Labour Organization (ILO) Convention 169 
on Indigenous and Tribal Peoples. The project aims to ensure that the rights of these groups are 
fully integrated into all aspects of its implementation. 
• Recognition  of  Rights:  The  Sercova  project  acknowledges  the  unique  rights  and  cultural 
identities of IPs and LCs. This includes respecting their land rights, traditional practices, and 
governance systems. The project will conduct comprehensive assessments to identify and 
document the customary rights of these communities, ensuring that their voices are heard 
and their rights are upheld throughout the project lifecycle. 
• Respect  for  Free,  Prior,  and  Informed  Consent  (FPIC):  In  alignment  with  UNDRIP  and  ILO 
Convention 169, the Sercova project will implement a robust framework for Free, Prior, and 
Informed Consent (FPIC). This framework ensures that IPs and LCs are fully informed about 
the project, its potential impacts, and benefits before any activities commence. The project 
will  engage  in  open  and  transparent  consultations,  allowing  communities  to  express  their 
views and make informed decisions about their involvement. 
• Promotion of Cultural Heritage and Livelihoods: The project will actively promote and protect 
the  cultural  heritage  and  traditional  practices  of  IPs  and  LCs.  This  includes  integrating 
traditional   ecological   knowledge   into   project   planning   and   implementation,   fostering 
sustainable  livelihoods  that  align  with  community  values.  By  supporting  local  capacity-

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
64 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
building initiatives and economic development opportunities, the project aims to empower 
these communities and enhance their resilience. 
• Mechanisms for  Grievance  and  Redress:  To  uphold human  rights,  the Sercova project  will 
establish  clear  mechanisms  for  grievance  and  redress.  This  will  provide  IPs,  LCs,  and 
customary rights holders with accessible channels to voice concerns regarding any violations 
of their rights. The project will ensure that grievances are addressed promptly and effectively, 
fostering trust and accountability in its operations. 
• Monitoring  and  Reporting:  The  Sercova  project  will  implement  a  robust  monitoring  and 
reporting  system  to  assess  its  compliance  with  human  rights  standards.  This  system  will 
include regular evaluations of community engagement processes, FPIC adherence, and the 
impact of project activities on IPs and LCs. Transparency in reporting will help ensure that 
stakeholders are informed about the project’s commitments and progress in respecting and 
promoting human rights. 
By adhering to these principles and practices, the Sercova project seeks to contribute positively to 
the rights and well-being of Indigenous Peoples, local communities, and customary rights holders, 
fostering a collaborative and respectful relationship that honors their inherent rights. 
2.5.4 Indigenous Peoples and Cultural Heritage (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
The Sercova project is committed to preserving and protecting the cultural heritage of Indigenous 
Peoples (IPs) as an integral part of its activities. Specifically, the project recognizes the importance 
of safeguarding the cultural legacy of the Indigenous community from the Seram tribe, a group with 
deep ancestral ties to their land and heritage. The project respects and acknowledges the cultural 
significance  of  the  traditional  sites  and  sacred  objects  that  form  an  essential  part  of  the  tribe's 
identity and history. 
• Preservation of Cultural Heritage: The Seram tribe possesses numerous customary areas that 
contain  valuable  cultural  heritage,  including  heirlooms  and  artifacts  passed  down  through 
generations. These cultural sites and objects represent the tribe’s historical connection to the 
land,  their  ancestors,  and  their  way  of  life.  As  part  of  the  project  activities,  measures  will  be 
taken to ensure that these areas and objects are not disturbed or damaged. The Sercova project 
will work closely with the community to map and document the locations of significant cultural 
sites and ensure their protection during project operations. 
• Protection of Ancestral Sites: In collaboration with the Seram tribe, the project will implement 
protective  measures around  ancestral  sites  to  safeguard  them  from  any  potential  impacts 
related  to  project  activities.  This  includes  creating  buffer  zones,  avoiding  project-related 
activities  in  culturally  sensitive  areas,  and  ensuring  that  the  community  leads  the  decisions 
regarding the management and care of these sites.  
• Community  Engagement  and  Respect  for  Traditions:  The  project  will  actively  engage  with  the 
Seram tribe to ensure that their cultural traditions and heritage are respected throughout the 
project. This includes ongoing consultations with the community’s elders and cultural leaders, 
ensuring that their perspectives are integrated into the planning and execution of the project. 
The  Sercova  project  will  also  provide  opportunities  for  community  members  to  participate  in 
cultural  heritage  preservation  activities,  ensuring  that  their  knowledge  and  practices  are 
maintained and celebrated. 
  
By preserving and protecting the cultural heritage of the Seram tribe, the SERCOVA project aims to 
not  only  minimize  disruption  but  also  promote  the  cultural  continuity  and  pride  of  Indigenous 
communities, ensuring that their legacy is honored and safeguarded for future generations. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
65 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.5.5 Statutory and Customary Property Rights (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
In line with applicable international human rights law and the United Nations Declaration on the 
Rights of Indigenous Peoples and ILO Convention 169 on Indigenous and Tribal Peoples, SERCOVA 
Project recognizes, respects, and promotes the protection of the rights of Indigenous People and 
Local  Communities  (IPLC) within  the SERCOVA  Project Area and Project  Zone.  Despite under the 
national  laws  and  regulations  the  entire  concession is under  the  right  holder  of  the  concession, 
SERCOVA Project always recognize the IPLC land use in the area. 
Statutory rights are represented by areas designated for project area, where formal legal rights for 
resource extraction or plantation activities are granted by the government. These rights coexist with 
community  claims,  necessitating  careful  management  to  respect  local  customs  and  protect 
community access.  
SERCOVA  Project  has  generated  an  initial  map  of  IPLC  land  and  resource  access  through 
participatory mapping following FPIC principle. The project has outlined land tenure, access, and 
management rights in the project zone, highlighting key areas under both statutory and customary 
authority. Village boundaries mark territories where local communities have customary control, with 
access and usage governed by traditional laws. Sacred areas, hunting grounds, and water springs 
are also clearly identified, signifying culturally significant sites that are managed by the community 
according to customary rules. All these areas are being further discussed with the communities on 
how to better manage the area for the benefit of the IPLC. The initial participatory map is shown in 
Figure 2-10. 
 
2.5.6 Recognition of Property Rights (VCS, 3.7, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
SERCOVA Project is committed to ensuring that all property rights within the project area are fully 
recognized,   respected,   and   supported.   This   includes   both   statutory  rights  granted   by   the 
government and customary rights held by local communities, particularly Indigenous Peoples and 
customary  rights  holders.  The  project  will  take  necessary  steps  to  ensure  that  land  tenure  and 
resource use are acknowledged in a way that benefits all stakeholders. 
The  project  will  ensure  that  formal  property  rights  (statutory  rights)  granted  by  the  government, 
such as licenses for company operations, are clearly defined and legally secured. At the same time, 
the project will work to respect and recognize the customary rights of local communities, which may 
include land for traditional practices, sacred sites, and natural resources. The project will ensure 
that these rights are protected throughout its implementation. 
Where  needed,  the SERCOVA project  will  take  proactive  steps  to  help  secure  statutory  property 
rights. This includes conducting thorough assessments to confirm legal boundaries, working with 
local  authorities  to  ensure  compliance  with  national  regulations,  and  engaging  in  transparent 
negotiations with landowners or rights holders. Additionally, conflict resolution mechanisms will be 
put  in  place  to  address  any  potential  disputes  over  land  or  resource  access,  ensuring  that  all 
property rights are fully recognized and protected. 
 
 
 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.5.7 Free, Prior and Informed Consent (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G5.2) 
The  SERCOVA  Project  actively  promotes  the  use  of  FPIC  principles.  Every  interaction  with  the 
community  follows  these  guidelines.  We  hold  open  and  transparent  discussions  during  all 
engagement activities, encouraging community members to voice any concerns about the project 
without  fear  of  reprisal.  We communicate  all  proposed  activities  to  the  communities  well  in 
advance, giving them ample time to discuss and raise objections. We view consent as an ongoing, 
multi-dimensional process, seeking it at various stages, hence multiple consents are expected as 
the results of FPIC process. Table below summarizes the process and outcome in obtaining consent 
from community.  
Table 2-19. The FPIC description and outcome process. 
Description of 
process for 
obtaining 
consent 
The process of obtaining consent for the implementation of project 
activities is carried out through structured phases involving indigenous 
communities, local communities, and customary rights holders to 
ensure that consent is free, prior, and based on informed 
understanding (FPIC). The first phase is a social, economic, and 
cultural study conducted in January 2024 to identify socio-economic 
conditions and map stakeholders in the village. The information from 
this study is used to provide a comprehensive understanding of the 
project's impacts to all parties involved. Following the social study, a 
public consultation was held in June 2024, involving local government 
and affected communities, to provide complete information about the 
project's objectives and impacts. Subsequently, village-level meetings 
were organized to obtain formal consent from the community, where 
they had the opportunity to engage in open discussions and reach a 
transparent agreement regarding the project's implementation. This 
process ensures that consent is given consciously and without 
coercion. 
Outcome of FPIC 
process 
The outcome of the FPIC process demonstrates that a transparent 
agreement has been reached between the concerned parties, 
including indigenous peoples, local communities, and customary rights 
holders. Prior to establishing this agreement, information regarding the 
project, including its objectives and potential impacts, was clearly 
disclosed to all involved parties. This ensured that all stakeholders had 
a comprehensive understanding and could provide informed consent. 
We assure that the project will not involve relocation of people without 
consent, will not encroach upon indigenous lands, and will not force 
physical or economic displacement. As part of our commitment, 
indigenous lands will be recognized and protected through project 
activities, and the community has expressed a desire to participate in 
forest and biodiversity protection initiatives. Furthermore, community 
input will be considered in developing social, educational, economic, 
and health programs that will be implemented alongside the project, 
ensuring sustainable benefits for all parties involved.

2.5.8 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (VCS, 3.18, 3.19;) 
The initial project concept was presented to the stakeholders respecting the principles and practice 
of community consultative process. Feedbacks were received and being discussed as an iterative 
and  consultative  process  between  SERCOVA  Project  and  surrounding  communities  particularly 
women, elderly, and other vulnerable communities (e.g., widow, orphan, etc.). The Benefit Sharing 
Plan  (BSP) is designed to  enabling  IPLC in  empowering  themselves,  and not as  recipients of aid 
and handouts. The BSP is being developed and tailored according to the village’s needs. 
Currently, community-initiative activities and development programs have being implemented by 
the project, benefiting IPLC within the Project Zone. In the long-run, the carbon finance component 
of  SERCOVA  Project  will  be  used  to  support  and  operate  the  overall  program  in  the  long-term, 
including the intensive 10-year long training and capacity building for communities.  
The  project  on  purpose  does  not  engage  in  cash  payments  to  farmers  which  could  distort  local 
economics and lead to jealousy and local conflict. Instead, community-initiative program that are 
biodiversity &   climate-friendly   are   welcomed   by   SERCOVA   Project   to   increase   community 
participation in the project, and to promote community-led initiative in climate action, biodiversity 
conservation, and social development. Table below shows the BSP development process.  
 
Table 2-20. The Benefit Sharing Plan (BSP) in SERCOVA Project.  
Process used to 
design the benefit 
sharing plan  
The process of developing the benefit-sharing plan was carried out 
through an inclusive and participatory approach, involving affected 
stakeholders, including indigenous communities, local communities, 
and relevant organizations. The first step in this process was to 
conduct needs and aspirations through interviews and focus group 
discussions. This approach allowed us to identify the priorities and 
aspirations of the community regarding the benefits they wish to 
receive from the project. 
After the initial data collection, a public consultation was held in June 
2024 involving all stakeholders to discuss and formulate the benefit-
sharing agreement. In this consultation, information about the 
potential benefits of the project, such as economic opportunities, 
education, and improvements in quality of life, was communicated to 
the community. Through open dialogue, all parties had the opportunity 
to provide input and adjust the benefit-sharing plan to better align with 
local needs. Subsequently, a final agreement was drafted based on the 
consultation results and will be reviewed periodically to ensure its 
effectiveness and sustainability as the project progresses. 
Currently, the BSP is being finalized to be tailored according to the 
villages’ needs through village-based FGD, meetings, and FPIC process. 
Summary of the 
benefit sharing 
plan 
The initial project design has been developed includes an agreement 
between the project proponent and the affected stakeholder groups, 
including indigenous peoples and local communities (IPLC). This 
agreement is designed to ensure that the benefits from the project are 
shared fairly and in accordance with the community's needs. The plan 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
68 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
encompasses various elements such as employment opportunities, 
skills training, support for social and educational programs, and 
contributions to health programs. 
Nevertheless, SERCOVA Project is till aim to get more detail expectation 
on BSP along the project time. Hence, the BSP will be added more, 
especially to include training and awareness to community resilient to 
climate change, and the establishment of “Wallace Center” for 
stakeholder information and knowledge hub. 
Approval and 
dissemination of 
benefit sharing 
plan 
The initial benefit-sharing agreement has been approved by the 
affected stakeholder groups through a transparent and participatory 
process. To ensure that all parties understand the contents of the 
agreement, the agreement document was discussed openly in 
meetings with the community, where explanations were provided in 
culturally appropriate language and context. This process allowed the 
community to provide input, ask questions about unclear aspects, and 
ensure that all elements of the agreement were well received. 
After obtaining approval, the benefit-sharing plan has been 
disseminated through various communication channels that are easily 
accessible to stakeholders, including sharing the agreement document 
collectively and providing information through notice boards in the 
village. The agreement document is available in appropriate formats 
and can be accessed by all interested parties, allowing them to review 
the agreement at any time. This effort aims to ensure transparency 
and encourage active community participation in monitoring the 
implementation of the agreement, thus fostering a sense of shared 
responsibility for the project's outcomes. 
Nevertheless, the BSP is still being finalized with an expectation to 
have an agreement with individual villages around SERCOVA Project 
Zone. 
 
2.5.9 Property Rights Protection (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.3) 
The SERCOVA project is committed to recognizing, respecting, and protecting the property rights of 
Indigenous Peoples (IPs), local communities (LCs), and customary rights holders. Specifically, the 
project acknowledges the property rights of the Seram tribe, the Indigenous population of Seram 
Island, who are protected under the regional regulations of Maluku Province. The project ensures 
that none of its activities will result in the involuntary removal or relocation of these property rights 
holders from their lands or territories, nor will it force them to move any activities crucial to their 
culture or livelihood. 
The project guarantees that all land and territory rights of the Seram tribe are safeguarded, and no 
relocation of their settlements or activities will take place as a result of project activities. Project 
planning and implementation are designed to respect the existing customs, traditions, and property 
rights of the IPs and LCs, ensuring that their way of life remains undisturbed. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
69 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In the event that any relocation of habitation or activities were to be necessary, it would only be 
undertaken with the free, prior, and informed consent (FPIC) of those affected. This process would 
also include just and fair compensation for any affected individuals or communities. However, in 
the current scope of the project, no relocations are required, and the rights of the Seram tribe and 
local communities remain intact. 
 
2.5.10 Illegal Activity Identification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G5.4) 
SERCOVA Project takes the identification and prevention of illegal activities seriously to ensure that 
its  impacts  are  not  negatively  affected  by  unlawful  actions.  Activities  such  as  illegal  logging, 
poaching, or unauthorized land use could potentially disrupt project objectives and undermine the 
sustainability  of  the  project.  The  project  has  established  mechanisms  to  monitor  and  report  any 
such activities, ensuring that all resources used and benefits derived from the project are free from 
illegal practices. 
To mitigate these risks, the project has implemented several measures aimed at reducing illegal 
activities. These include collaborating with local authorities, community awareness programs, and 
regular monitoring of the project area to detect and address any unauthorized activities. By working 
closely with local communities and government bodies, the project ensures that all operations are 
transparent and in line with legal and ethical standards. 
Additionally,  the SERCOVA Project  is  fully  committed  to  upholding  human  rights.  The  project 
guarantees that no victims of human trafficking, forced labor, or child labor are or will be involved 
in any aspect of the project. Strict labor policies and monitoring procedures are in place to ensure 
that all individuals working on the project are employed under fair and legal conditions, with respect 
for their rights and dignity. 
 
2.5.11 Ongoing Disputes (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.5) 
SERCOVA project  is  committed  to  ensuring  that  no  unresolved  conflicts  or  disputes  affect  its 
operations.  In  2018,  there  was  a  conflict  between  the  company  and  several  villages  concerning 
land  and  resource  rights.  However,  this  dispute  has  been  fully  resolved  through  a  fair  and 
transparent  process,  ensuring  that  all  parties  reached  an  agreement  in  accordance  with  local 
customs  and  regulations.  The  resolution  of  this  dispute  has  been  documented  and  no  further 
issues have arisen since then. 
To maintain neutrality, the project ensures that no activity will be undertaken that could influence 
or prejudice the outcome of any unresolved disputes related to the project area. This includes a 
careful review of any historical land or resource conflicts to prevent interference with local rights or 
customs. 
If any new disputes were to arise, SERCOVA project has designed measures to address and resolve 
conflicts in a fair and constructive manner. These include open consultations with stakeholders, 
conflict resolution mechanisms involving local authorities and customary leaders, and ensuring the 
free, prior, and informed consent (FPIC) of all parties involved.

Additionally,  local  customary  institutions  and  traditional  leaders  have  given  their  consent,

2.6 Additional Information Relevant to the Project  
2.6.1 Leakage Management (VCS, 3.11, 3.15) 
To  manage  leakage  and  implementation  risk  mitigation  measures  based  on  the  VM0010 
methodology and VCS standard 4.7, as follows: 
2.6.1.1 Leakage Management Plan 
Identification of leakage sources involves examining various aspects of potential emission shifts. 
Activity  shifting  leakage  requires  identifying  activities  that  might  be  displaced  as  a  result  of 
project  implementation,  such  as  logging  operations  moving  to  other  areas.  Market  leakage 
concerns the potential increase in emissions from other regions due to reduced supply from the 
project  area. Therefore,  it  is  necessary  to  assess  the  emissions  rise  in  these  other  areas. 
Quantification of leakage includes calculating the emissions from displaced activities according 
to the guidelines provided by the VM0010 methodology and determining the emissions resulting 
from increased production elsewhere. To ensure precise net reductions, it is essential to subtract 
the quantified leakage from the total emission reductions. 
2.6.1.2 Implementation of Leakage and Risk Mitigation Measures 
Preventive  measures  include  establishing  buffer  zones  around  the  project  area  to  avert  the 
displacement of activities and engaging with local communities to provide alternative livelihoods, 
thereby  reducing  the  risk  of  such  displacement.  Monitoring  and  reporting  require  ongoing 
vigilance, with continuous monitoring in both the project area and surrounding regions to identify 
any leakage activities, complemented by transparent reporting of findings as part of the project's 
monitoring  plan  to  maintain  accountability.  Adaptive  management  is pivotal,  incorporating 
feedback  mechanisms  to  adjust  project  activities  based  on  monitoring  results  and  conducting 
regular risk assessments to update mitigation measures as needed. 
2.6.2 Further Information  
SERCOVA  project  is  not  located  within  a  jurisdiction  covered  by  a  jurisdictional  REDD+  program. 
Until  now,  there  are  two  jurisdictional  REDD+  program  at  provincial  level  signed  by  the  national 
government (Ministry of Environment and Forestry, MoEF) under the national REDD+ program. Both 
jurisdictional REDD+ programs are funded by the World Bank, namely the East Kalimantan Province 
(East Kalimantan Jurisdictional Emission Reductions Program, EK JERP
35
) and the Jambi Province 
(Bio Carbon Fund Integrated Sustainable Forest Landscape, BioCF ISFL
36
).  
 
At national level, Indonesia has received Result-based Payment (RBP) for emission reduction within 
period  2014 – 2016  from  Green  Climate  Fund  (GCF)  for  USD  103.8  million,  and  period  2016 – 
2017 from Indonesia-Norway Partnership for USD 56 million
37
. Other than those programs, there 
are no other jurisdictional REDD+ program has been signed or implemented in Indonesia. Hence, 
SERCOVA Project that is located in Maluku Province and started in year 2021, is not covered by a 
jurisdictional REDD+ program. 
                                                 
35
 https://www.forestcarbonpartnership.org/country/indonesia 
36
 https://biocf.jambiprov.go.id/ 
37
 https://ppid.menlhk.go.id/berita/siaran-pers/7638/menlhk-indonesia-menjadi-contoh-internasional-dalam-redd-dan-rbp-emisi-karbon 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
73 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Furthermore, as part of the Indonesia regulation and policies, all forestry permit holder must make 
an  Action  Plan  Document  to  Mitigate  climate  change,  called  DRAM  (Dokumen  Rencana  Aksi 
Mitigasi) which  is required by the company’s General Management Plan (Rencana  Kerja Umum, 
RKU). This  DRAM  must  also  be  submitted  under  the  National  Registry  System  (Sistem  Registri 
Nasional, SRN) as a record of the action. Despite the document is a government-led initiative, the 
activities conducted by the Project in the RKU is solely made by the project’s efforts including the 
technicality,  resources,  funds,  including  project  implementation  with  community.  The  DRAM  is 
considered as part of the licensing process in forestry permit.  
 
DRAM was  established  under  the  Presidential  Regulation  No.  98/2021  on  the  Carbon  Pricing 
Mechanism. For  over  three years (2021 – 2024), none  of DRAM  made by  forestry company has 
generated carbon  unit  that  equal  to  a  tradeable  carbon  unit  or  called  SPE-GRK  (Sertifikat 
Penurunan  Emisi-Gas  Rumah  Kaca). This  situation  demonstrates that none  of  the  DRAM  from 
Forestry sector are meant to become a carbon project which produce carbon unit that can generate 
carbon revenue to implement climate change mitigation and adaptation. Instead, DRAM was made 
to  record  what  is  going  to  be  done  by  the  license  holder,  and  how  that  can  be  counted  as  host-
country contribution to climate change.  
 
Nevertheless,  SERCOVA  Project  is  following  the  government  regulation  and  submitted  DRAM  as 
part  of the forestry  license  requirement.  SERCOVA Project  do not  seek  credits from SRN  or SPE-
GRK. SERCOVA Project is neither registered under any other GHG program that generate carbon 
credit nor included in an emissions trading program or binding emission limit that provide carbon 
revenue to implement project activities. Hence, all GHG emission reduction or removal generated 
from  SERCOVA  Project  is  solely  due  to  SERCOVA  actions  in  project  implementation,  fund,  and 
resources complying with the Indonesia regulation and policies.

3.1.4 Baseline Scenario (VCS, 3.13) 
According  to  the  Baseline  Scenario  identification  procedures  in  the  VM0010 Methodology  for 
Improved Forest Management refers to VCS tool VT0001 for the Demonstration and Assessment 
of  Additionality in  Agricultural,  Forestry  and  Other  Land  Use  (AFOLU),  the  following  steps  are 
followed to identify the most plausible baseline scenario in the project. 
3.1.4.1 Step 1. Identification of alternative land use scenarios to the proposed IFM project 
Sub-step 1a) Identify and list all credible alternative land use scenarios 
According  to  VM0010  Methodology  for  Improved  Forest  Management (Version  1.3)  project 
activities are referred for guidance on identifying realistic and credible alternative land uses. The 
identified  land  use  scenarios  shall  at  least  include: i)  Continuation  of  pre-project  land  use, ii) 
project activity within the land of project boundary performed as non-registered the VCS AFOLU 
project, iii) if applicable, activities similar to the proposed project activity on at least part of the 
land within the project boundary of the proposed VCS AFOLU project at a rate resulting from legal 
requirements or extrapolation of observed similar activities in the geographical area with similar 
socioeconomic and ecological conditions to the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity occurring in 
the period beginning ten years prior to the project start date. The following scenarios (outcome 
sub-step 1a) could occur on the land within the proposed project boundary in the absence of the 
AFOLU project activity:

Sub-step 1b) Checking the consistency of credible land use scenarios with enforced mandatory 
applicable laws and regulations 
As it stated earlier, the project area was designated as the production forest (timber harvesting) 
before  starting  the  project  activity,  scenarios  ii and iii identified  in  sub-step  1a  are  legally 
permissible under the current laws and regulations of the Ministry of Environment and Forestry. 
Albeit  the potential hydrocarbon  exploration (scenario i) within  the  project area has a  potential 
as it revealed by Ministry of Energy and Mineral Resources of Indonesia, this scenario requires 
new permit as changing the status from forest logging to mining. Thus, the outcome of this sub-
step 1b includes all the scenarios identified in the table above. 
Sub-step 1c) Select the most plausible baseline scenario 
Given the historical context and the current legal framework, a combination of continued logging, 
potential  HTI  establishment,  and  expanded  hydrocarbon  and  mining  activities  represents  the 
most likely baseline scenario. These activities, combined with the area’s vulnerability to flooding, 
forest  fires,  and  illegal  logging,  make  a  strong  case  that,  without  the  project,  the  land  would 
experience significant environmental degradation. As the baseline contains six scenarios, further 
stepwise analyses are required to justify the selection and determination of the most plausible 
scenarios. Thus, all scenarios are included to identify the barrier analysis. To determine the most 
likely baseline scenario and show additionality of the project activity, a barrier analysis (Step 3 
VT0001) was applied. 
 
3.1.4.2 Step 3. Barrier analysis 
According  to  VM0010  Methodology  for  Improved  Forest  Management  (Version  1.3),  identifying 
realistic and credible alternative land uses as a result of sub-step 1b and 1c. A barrier analysis 
was  conducted  to identify  realistic  and  credible  barriers  that  prevent  implementation  of  these 
land use scenarios, following the procedures in Step 3 of VT0001 Tool for the Demonstration and 
Assessment of Additionality in VCS AFOLU project activities.

Sub-step 3a) Identification of barriers 
Table 3-6. Identification of barriers 
Identified Barriers Description 
1 
Legal/regulatory 
barrier 
Complex legal frameworks that require navigating multiple permits 
or   approvals. The   transition   from   a   logging   concession   to   a 
protected forest requires significant legal and regulatory changes, 
including securing PBPH and MUK permits. 
2 Ecological barrier 
Degradation  of  natural  resources,  such  as  soil  and  water,  could 
pose   challenges   for   project   success. Moreover,   the  area’s 
vulnerability   to   flooding   and   forest   fires   presents   significant 
challenges to maintaining forest integrity and carbon stocks. 
3 Market barrier 
The  absence  of  strong  market  incentives  for  forest  conservation, 
coupled  with  the  financial  attractiveness  of  logging, industrial 
plantation,   and  mining  activities,   creates   substantial  barriers. 
Additionally, Price fluctuations in commodities (e.g., timber, oil) can 
affect the financial viability of conservation initiatives. 
4 
Operational 
barrier 
The  presence  of  illegal  logging  activities  and  the  expansion  of 
mining   in   riparian   zones   would   make   forest   protection   more 
challenging. 
5 Social barrier 
Resistance  from  local  communities  or  indigenous  groups  who 
depend on the forest for their livelihoods could create conflicts and 
delays in development of several land use scenarios. Differing land 
use  interests  or  concerns  over  livelihoods  may  lead  to  differing 
perspectives   from local   communities   or   stakeholders. Land 
ownership    or    use    conflicts,    particularly    in    areas    involving 
indigenous    or    marginalized    populations,    requiring    careful 
management. 
6 Financial barrier 
Challenges  in  securing  sufficient  financing,  particularly  for  large-
scale  or  long-term  conservation  projects. In  addition,  there  are 
lower financial returns compared to alternative land uses, making 
it less attractive to potential investors. 
7 
Institutional 
barrier 
The  need  for  strong  governance  and  institutional  capacity  to 
support    sustainable    land    management    practices. Ongoing 
collaboration  with  government  entities  to  align  policy  incentives 
with conservation and sustainable land use goals.

Table 3-7. Plausible scenario against identified barriers.  
Scenario 
Barriers 
i
. 
Expansion of hydrocarbon 
exploration 
This  scenario  faces  institutional,  technological,  and 
ecological  barriers.  For  the  institutional  barriers,  the 
permit  of  land  use  within  the  project  area  will  change 
the policies and laws since the PT SP holds the license 
with   a   legal   right   to   conduct   harvesting   timber 
products. To  explore  hydrocarbons,  companies  must 
obtain   an   Exploration   Permit   from   the   Ministry   of 
Energy  and  Mineral  Resources  (MEMR).  If  the  land  is 
categorized as state forest, approval from the Ministry 
of Environment and Forestry (MoEF) is also required. If 
the  exploration  or  exploitation  activities  are  to  be 
conducted  in  state  forest  land,  the  company  must 
obtain a Forest Area Utilization Permit (IPPKH) from the 
MoEF.  This  permit  allows  the  temporary  use  of  forest 
land  for  non-forestry  purposes,  such  as  hydrocarbon 
exploration  and  exploitation. The  IPPKH  is  subject  to 
certain  conditions,  such  as  payment  of  forest  area 
utilization   fees   and   reforestation   obligations. After 
successful   exploration,   if   the   company   wishes   to 
proceed with exploitation (production), it must obtain a 
Production Permit (IUP Operasi Produksi) from MEMR. 
This  permit  authorizes  the  company  to  extract  and 
produce  hydrocarbons. The  company  must  also  seek 
an  extension  or  renewal  of  the  IPPKH  from  MoEF  to 
continue using the forest land for production purposes. 
The  company  must  comply  with  all  relevant  forestry 
and    environmental    laws    during    exploration    and 
exploitation   activities.   This   includes   implementing 
measures to minimize environmental impact, 
conducting   regular   monitoring,   and   fulfilling   any 
reforestation or land rehabilitation obligations. Regular 
audits  and  inspections  by  government  authorities will 
take    place    to    ensure compliance    with    these 
regulations. At  the  end  of  the  production  period,  the 
company  is  responsible  for  rehabilitating  the land  to 
restore  it  to  its  original  condition  or  to  a  condition 
agreed upon with the government. Finally, the IPPKH is 
revoked, and the land is returned to the state, usually 
under a reforestation or conservation program. 
Moreover,  the  exploration  of  oil  requires  technology 
investment to consider  the potential of the amount  of

3.1.5 Additionality (VCS, 3.14) 
In compliance with the methodology's requirements, the project's additionality is demonstrated 
utilizing  the  most  current  version  of  the  VCS  tool VT0001 designed  for  the  demonstration  and 
assessment  of  additionality  in  AFOLU  project  activities. Following  the  VT0001,  the  project’s 
eligibility, including the legal rights and intent to harvest, has been thoroughly reviewed. Since no 
barriers prevent this scenario, continued commercial logging is deemed the most likely baseline 
scenario. By halting this logging, the project takes a substantial step forward. It actively enhances 
carbon sequestration, protects biodiversity, and delivers social benefits, moving decisively from 
the  baseline  scenario  towards  genuine  environmental  sustainability. Hence,  the  project  is 
deemed additional.  
Furthermore, the alternative land use scenarios to the proposed project is explained in Chapter 
3.1.4. Baseline Scenario, and the steps following the additionality methods are detailed below: 
3.1.5.1 Regulatory Surplus (VCS, 3.14) 
Is the project located in an UNFCCC Annex 1 or Non-Annex 1 country? 
☐   Annex 1 country  ☒   Non-Annex 1 country 
Are the project activities mandated by any law, statute, or other regulatory framework?

If the project is located inside a Non-Annex 1 country and the project activities are mandated by 
a law, statute, or other regulatory framework, are such laws, statutes, or regulatory frameworks 
systematically enforced?  
☐  Yes   ☐  No 
Project activity of Logged to Protected Forest (LtPF) is not mandated by any law, statute, or 
other regulatory framework. Logging license holders are not mandated to convert their activities 
from logging to protection forest.  
3.1.5.2 Additionality Methods (VCS, 3.14) 
In compliance with the methodology's requirements, the project's additionality is demonstrated 
utilizing  the  most  current  version  of  the  VCS  tool VT0001 designed  for  the  demonstration  and 
assessment of additionality in AFOLU project activities. 
Step 1. Identification of alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity 
For  this  step,  please  refer  to section 3.1.4.1 as the  methodology  has  confirmed  the  possible 
baseline scenarios. 
Step 2. Investment Analysis 
VT0001 provides the option to undertake either Step 2 (Investment analysis) or Step 3 (Barrier 
analysis).  Step  3  was  selected  to  be  used  to  determine  the  baseline  and  additionality. 
Nevertheless, the project undertook an analysis of the Net Present Value (NPV) between baseline 
scenario  and  project  scenario,  which  showed  that continued  logging  (scenario  ii) is  the  most 
profitable land use compared to the project scenario (Appendix 19). The barriers to the alternative 
scenarios and the project scenario are evaluated in the following section. As established in this 
section, the most likely and credible business-as usual scenario is the continued logging.  
 
Step 3. Barrier Analysis 
For this step, please refer to section 3.1.4.2. 
 
Step 4. Common Practice Analysis 
In  many  similar  areas (production  and  limited  production  forest),  logging and HTI  development 
are more common practices, whereas forest conservation is less typical, further reinforcing the 
project’s additionality. The hydrocarbon  exploration  and/or  exploitation is  plausible  when the 
area of  state-forest  land is  granted an approval by the Ministry  of  Forestry  and  Environment of 
Indonesia Without  carbon  finance  revenues,  the  practice  of  protecting  intact  forests  on 
production  forest  land  in  Indonesia  is  rare.  It  is  uncommon  to  maintain  intact  forests  in  these 
areas,  as  such  efforts  typically  depend  on donor  or  corporate  funding,  which  is  often  limited. 
Similar  conservation  and  restoration  projects  in  Maluku,  mostly  for aquatic conservation  area, 
are usually carried out by non-profit organizations and rely on philanthropic support.  
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
86 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Conclusion 
Under VT0001, the project’s eligibility, including the legal rights and intent to harvest, has been 
thoroughly  reviewed.  Since  no  barriers  prevent  this  scenario,  continued  commercial  logging  is 
deemed the most likely baseline scenario. By halting this logging, the project takes a substantial 
step forward. It actively enhances carbon sequestration, protects biodiversity, and delivers social 
benefits,   moving   decisively   from   the   baseline   scenario   towards   genuine   environmental 
sustainability.

This is not applicable as there are no methodological deviations in the project. 
 
3.2 Quantification of Estimated GHG Emission Reductions and Removals 
3.2.1 Baseline Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
3.2.1.1 Section 6.1 Selection of the baseline 
Please refer to Section 3.1.4 and 3.1.5 above. 
3.2.1.2 Section 6.2 Modelling of the baseline scenario 
PT Strata Pacific concession which spans approximately ±73,365 hectares in the Balkuriti, Bubi, 
and Baba Forest Groups. This area is administratively under the Bula Sub-district, Bula West Sub-
district and Teluk Waru Sub-district, East Seram District, Maluku Province. PT Strata Pacific holds 
forest   utilization   business   permit   (Perizinan   Berusaha   Pemanfaatan   Hutan/PBPH)   since 
September   2021   through   the   Decree   of   Minister   of   Environment   and   Forestry   Number 
SK.687/MenLHK/Setjen/  HPL.0/9/2021.  This  is  an  updated  license  from  logging  concession 
permit  (IUPHHK-HA)  in  2013  which  granted  through  Decree  of  Minister  of  Environment  and 
Forestry  Number  SK.774/Menhut-II/2013.  PT  Strata  Pacific  has  been  granted  the  approval  on 
Work Plan for The Utilisation of Timber Forest Products in Natural Forest Within Production Forest 
Based on Periodic Comprehensive Forest Inventory (IHMB) for the period 2016 to 2025 based on 
Indonesia   MoEF   decree   SK.2088/MenLHK-PHPL/UHP/HPL.1/5/2016.   These   licenses   and 
decree act as government-proven records for forest management license with more than 5 years 
preceding  the  project  start  date  and  that  the  management  practices  have  surpassed  the  legal 
requirement. Additionally, historical records of prior harvesting activities by PT Strata Pacific took 
place in 2018 to 2019. Unfortunately, due to Covid-19 pandemic, no timber harvesting activities 
occurred since early 2020. Therefore, the “historical baseline scenario" option is eligible to be 
used for modelling the baseline scenario. 
Step 1: Forest Volume Inventory 
The  method  used  for  vegetation  data  collection  follows  the  Indonesian  National  Standard  (SNI) 
7724:2011 (SNI7724,  2011a) and  IHMB  (Ministry  of  Forestry Regulation  of  the  Republic  of 
Indonesia No P.33/Menhut-II/2009, 2009) with some modifications. Forest carbon assessment is 
conducted using a two-kilometer transect line, each containing five nested plots sized 125 meters 
x 20 meters (see Figure 3-1). The placement of the transect line is clustered/stratified based on 
land cover classes and randomly placed within every land cover class. The estimation on sampling 
plot  needed  for  the  carbon  assessment  is  calculated  using  Winrock’s  CDM  A/R  Sample  Plot 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
87 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Calculator   Tool (Walker,   2014) which   applies   the   equation   from   the   UNFCCC   A/R   CDM 
methodological tool version 2.1 (UNFCCC, 2010). SERCOVA estimates the number of plots within 
the  range  of  10%  error,  95%  confidence  interval,  and  20%  additional  plots  (minimum  10%)  as 
suggested  by  the  tool.    A  total  of  19  sampling  plots  (0.25  ha  each)  were  needed  to  fulfil  the 
requirements. This study used 30 sampling plots (0.25 ha each) on a total of six transect lines for 
collecting carbon accounting data and deemed sufficient to fulfil the methodology requirements.  
 
Figure 3-1 Nested sampling plot for biomass estimation 
 
There  are  three  plot  sizes  used  for  three  vegetation  growth  types  based  on  diameter  at  breast-
height (DBH): 125 m x 20 m plot for tree level (DBH≥30cm), 20 m x 20 m plot for pole level 
(15cm≥DBH>30cm), and 10 m x 10 m plot for sapling level (5cm≥DBH>15cm). Within each sub-
plot, the parameters recorded are tree species, DBH per individual tree according to its DBH class, 
and tree height. Coordinates for each plot are recorded using Garmin™ GPS type 65s at the starting 
point of the plot. Each recorded tree is marked with numbered aluminum sheets which are carefully 
nailed into each tree.  Leaves, branches, fruits, and flowers of each tree species are collected to 
create  the  herbarium.  Each  herbarium  specimen  is  photographed  for  further  identification 
purposes.  Initial  plant  species  identification  is  done  in  the  field.  To  confirm  the  identified  plant 
species, the herbarium specimens and collected photos are cross verified using plant identification 
key  guidebooks  and  matched  with  the  available  plant  photo  database  and  existing  herbarium 
specimens.  To  complete  the  vegetation  species  list,  conservation  status,  density,  and  obtain 
comprehensive habitat descriptions in the study area, exploratory observations are also conducted 
around the nested plots and observer-transversed paths. This vegetation data is also used to verify 
secondary  data  collected  on  ecosystem  types  (Eco  floristics).  Specimen  nomenclature  and 
taxonomy follow the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group IV (Stevens, 2017). A more detailed description 
is available for validation in the Standard Operational Procedure. The sampling plots distribution is 
shown in Figure 3-2 below. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 3-2 Map of the transect and sampling plots location in SERCOVA project area 
Forest carbon estimation is carried out using a non-destructive sampling approach. Wood density 
values  are  obtained  from  the  Global  Wood  Density  Database (Chave,  et  al.,  2009),  where  the 
default IPCC value of 0.66 g/cm
3
 is used for tree species not found in the database list. From the 
measured  DBH,  species  identification,  species  wood  density  and  tree  heights,  we  then  use  the 
allometric equations available and compare based on the highest correlations to choose the most 
suitable  allometric  for  the  biomass  analysis.  SERCOVA  compared  four  allometric  equations 
including (Brown, 1997), (Ketterings, 2001), (Chave, 2014) and (Manuri, 2017) in Table 3-8 below.

The tree form factor value, known as "angka bentuk pohon" (F), is 0.81. This figure is derived from 
actual timber harvested by PT Strata Pacific in 2018 and 2019, comparing the mean tree diameter 
at the bottom (Diameter Pangkal, DP) and the mean tree diameter at the top (Diameter Ujung, DU). 
Merchantable  timber  volume at  the plot  level  is calculated by  summing  the timber  volume of all 
trees exceeding the defined cutting limits: a minimum diameter at breast height (DBH) of 50 cm. 
Moreover,  this  calculation  only  includes  all  species  listed  in  the  Indonesia  Ministry  of  Forestry 
Decree on the Grouping of Timber Species as a Basis for Imposing Forestry Fees (Kep-Menhut-163-
2003). Based on the FAO Global Forest Resources Assessment  2020 for Indonesia, the growing 
stock level for the Maluku province in Secondary Dryland Forest (Hutan Lahan Kering Sekunder) is

91 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
60.25  m³  ha⁻¹.  According  to  the  IPCC  2006  INV  GLs  AFOLU  Chapter  4  Table  4.5,  the  biomass 
conversion and expansion factor applicable to wood removal (BCEFR) is a 1.89-ton dry mass m⁻³.  
Step 2: Demarcation of All Non-Harvest Areas 
The non-harvest areas specified within the Business License for the Utilization of Timber Forest 
Products in Natural Forests (Izin Usaha Pemanfaatan Hasil Hutan Kayu Dalam Hutan, IUPHHK) for 
PT SP, for the period from 2016 to 2025, are categorized as follows: 
1. Protection Zones: These areas are designated for environmental conservation and include: 
• A Buffer Zone of 4,031 ha, which is intended to reduce the impacts of logging activities on 
adjacent ecosystems. 
• A Riparian Zone spanning 1,234 ha, which is essential for protecting water bodies and 
maintaining aquatic habitats. 
• A Germplasm Conservation Area of 315 ha, dedicated to the preservation of plant genetic 
resources. 
• A Wildlife Conservation Area covering 300 ha, which aims to protect habitats critical for 
various wildlife species. 
2. Non-Effective  Production  Areas:  These  areas  are  set  aside  for  purposes  other  than  timber 
extraction and include: 
• A Seed Garden of 600 ha, used for growing and preserving tree seeds. 
• Permanent Sampling Plots of 600 ha, designated for research and monitoring of forest 
conditions. 
• Facilities  and  Infrastructure  occupying  809 ha,  which  encompasses  various  support 
structures necessary for operations. 
These non-harvest areas constitute 10.7% (7,337 ha) of the total PT SP concession area, which 
measures 73,365 ha.

Step 4: Design and Presentation of Forestry Infrastructure 
Forest Area Opening (Pembukaan Wilayah Hutan, PWH) involved creating infrastructure for timber 
production  and  forest  management  during  the  management  period.  Its  goal  was  to  build  roads, 
bridges, culverts, and landings to facilitate timber transport and improve forest oversight. Existing 
functional  infrastructure  was  maintained  and  further  developed,  while  new  infrastructure  was 
planned  and  built  as  needed. The  construction  of  roads,  bridges,  and  culverts  was  essential  for 
transporting  timber  and  managing  the  forest.  These  roads  were  also  crucial  for  post-logging 
management, forest protection, and general supervision. Main and branch roads were kept in good 
condition to meet their intended functions. This PWH includes: 
Road Density / Intensity PWH  
IPWH  measured  the  ratio  of  road  length  (in  meters)  to  the  area  of  a  production  unit  (in ha). 
Economically,  optimal  IPWH  occurred  when  the  cost  of  road  construction  equalled  the  cost  of 
maintenance. Key factors included timber volume, desired road quality, and construction costs. On 
average, 300 meters of road was needed for a 100-ha logging area. With an efficiency factor of 6 
for hilly terrain, the calculated road density was 20 meters per ha. Consequently, the IPWH intensity 
was  20  meters  per ha,  with  8  meters  per ha allocated  to  main  roads  and  12  meters  per ha to 
branch roads. 
Road Construction activities 
Road construction encompassed planning, road tracing, measurement, and mapping, along with 
the building and hardening of roads, including the construction of bridges, culverts, and ditches. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
93 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Main roads were designed for year-round use and required hardening, while branch and spur roads 
generally did not, except in areas prone to landslides or steep slopes. 
The construction process involved several stages: first, the road route was marked, followed by the 
clearing of vegetation and debris. Topsoil, typically 10-20 cm thick, was removed from the roadbed. 
The  roadbed  was  then  shaped  through  excavation  and  filling,  graded  and  leveled  to  smooth  the 
surface,  and  initially  compacted.  For  main  roads,  stones  were  collected  for  surfacing,  and 
hardening  materials  were  handled,  spread,  and  leveled.  A  final  compaction  was  performed  to 
complete the roadbed. 
Additional infrastructure included bridges, culverts, ditches, and road signage. The planned road 
density was 20 meters per ha, with 8 meters per ha for main roads and 12 meters per ha for branch 
roads. Spur roads were planned to have up to 22 meters per ha. Existing roads were utilized and 
improved as needed, with new construction completed at least six months before logging. The 10-
year road construction planning (2016-2025) was detailed in table below. 
Table 3-10. The 10-year road construction (RKU 2016 – 2025).

Construction of Base Camps, TPn/TPK, and Logponds 
The construction of operational control centers for forest management, including base camps, was 
tailored  to  the  distribution  of  logging  areas  and  the  accessibility  of  production  and  forest 
management activities. The base camps constructed were either permanent or semi-permanent. 
For  routine  operational  activities,  such  as  RKT  (Annual  Work  Plan),  temporary  camps  were 
established according to the position of the RKT blocks. 
• Main  Camp:  This  was  the  primary  or  central  camp,  serving  as  the  hub  for  forest 
management activities. 
• Forest   Management   Camp:   This   camp   focused   on   forest   management   activities, 
particularly related to seedling production. 
• Community  Development  Camp:  This  camp  was  dedicated  to  community  development 
activities around the forest. 
• Log pond Camp: This camp was the administrative center for TUK (Log Collection Centers), 
primarily handling logs being prepared for industry. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
94 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Wood  collection  areas  included  TPn  (temporary  wood  collection  points)  and  TPK  (wood  storage 
areas). The construction of these wood collection sites was intended to serve the entire production 
area planned for each year, with locations determined based on the logging blocks. A TPn with an 
area  of  approximately  0.5 ha and  an  average  distance  of  250  meters  was  estimated  to  serve  a 
logging  area  of  about  100 ha.  Therefore,  to  serve  a  single  logging  block  of  1,868 ha,  19  TPn, 
covering a total area of 9.5 ha, were required.

of forestry infrastructure, differs between species.ha⁻¹ for stratum VF and 48.95 m³ ha⁻¹ for s. 
The  merchantable  volume  per  hectare  per  species,  or  the  mean volume  of  timber  extracted  for 
wood processing during the establishment of forestry infrastructure, differs between species. The 
total values are 79.52 m³ ha⁻¹ for stratum VF and 48.95 m³ ha⁻¹ for stratum LOA. 
Not all harvested biomass leaves the forest area, as timber harvested consists of two components: 
wood  removed  to  market  (extracted  timber)  and  wood  remaining  in  the  forest  post-harvest. The 
fraction of merchantable timber volume from clearing of forest roads, skid trails and log landings 
that is to  be processed  into  wood  products (F
V,INF,HWP
) is calculated from  the forest  management 
records by dividing the sum of volume of trees per hectare with DBH ≥ 50 cm with the sum of all 
volume  of  trees  per  hectare. The  calculated  value  of  F
V,INF,HWP
 is  71.6%. Hence,  the total 
merchantable  volume  per  hectare for  all species J of  timber  not  extracted  for  wood  processing 
during  the  establishment  of  forestry  infrastructure (V
notEX,INF,j,i|BSL
) values  are  31.52  m³  ha⁻¹ for 
stratum VF and 19.41 m³ ha⁻¹ for stratum LOA as shown in Table 3-16 below.

3.2.1.6 Section 8.1.2 Calculation of dead wood (logging slash) generated in the process of 
timber harvest 
As permitted by the VCS AFOLU Requirements, the deadwood is assumed to be left on the forest 
floor following timber harvest (10-year decay function). Thus, the change in carbon stock in dead 
wood is  estimated  as  the  difference between  the  total carbon  stock  of  the  harvested  biomass 
(C
HB,j,i|BSL
) and the carbon stock of the extracted timber (C
EX,j,i|BSL
), including the residual stand

) and  biomass  of  trees  left  to  decay  as  a  result  forestry  infrastructure 
establishment (C
notHB,inf,j,i|BSL
), as follows (VM0010 Equation 5):

3.2.1.7 Section 8.1.3 Calculation of baseline carbon sequestered in wood products 
In  every  instance  where  wood  is  collected  to  be  turned  into  wood  products,  the  carbon  stock 
within  those  wood  products  must  be  accounted  for  in  the  baseline  scenario,  thus  the  carbon 
stock in the wood products pool included. The carbon stock of extracted timber across species 
(C
EX,j,i|BSL
) is calculated as refers to the Table 3-18.  
The  methodology  differentiates  between 1)  wood  products  that  will  decompose  within  3  years 
after harvest (i.e., Wood Waste (WW) and Short-Lived Fraction (SLF)), 2) wood products that are 
discarded between 3 and 100 years after harvest, and 3) other wood products that are deemed

3.2.1.9 Section 8.1.5 Calculation of baseline emissions from the combustion of fossil fuels in 
forestry and wood processing machinery 
Carrying  out  the  baseline  activity  (e.g. selective  logging  or  clearcutting)  involves  using  forestry 
and wood processing machinery, which generates greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions due to fossil 
fuel combustion. The combustion from wood processing machinery was not calculated as there 
were no such an activity by PT SP (e.g. only roundwood product). However, the combustion from 
fossil  fuel  from harvesting  operation,  log  hauling  and  log  transport were  calculated  to  identify 
whether significance or insignificance. The significance test result from the combustion of fossil 
fuels  in  forestry was 3.3%. According  to  the  VM0010  methodology  section  5.4, the  sum  of 
decreases in carbon pools and increases in emissions that may be neglected must be less than 
5% of the total project GHG benefits, thus this excluded. To align with the methodology, any GHG 
emissions from these sources are not accounted for, as this approach is deemed conservative. 
Consequently, these emissions are excluded from the calculations, and C
Fuel
 is set to zero in the 
baseline. 
3.2.1.10    Section 8.1.6 Calculation of baseline scenario greenhouse gas emissions from change 
in carbon stocks
