emissions is equal to the carbon stock change as a result of timber harvest plus the carbon stock 
change resulting from conversion and retirement of wood products minus carbon sequestration 
from forest regrowth after harvest.

years since the start of the project activity were calculated, using the following equations:

3.2.2 Project Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
In accordance with the applicability conditions of VM0010 (referring to the minor revision on 10 
June 2024), The increase in carbon stocks due to ongoing forest growth can only be attributed to 
a project in cases where trees would have been harvested under the baseline scenario. Growth 
in areas and trees not subject to harvest in the baseline scenario would have occurred regardless 
of  project  implementation  and  cannot  be  credited  to  the  project. As  a  result,  carbon  stock 
changes due to vegetation management and fuel removal will be negligible. Thus, net greenhouse 
gas emissions in the project scenario will be equal to carbon sequestration through ongoing forest 
growth  minus  any  emissions  resulting  from  forest  disturbance  (both  illegal  logging  and  natural 
disturbances). 
3.2.2.1 Section 8.2.1 Calculation of Ongoing Forest growth in the project scenario 
The annual change in carbon stock in the aboveground biomass of trees in the project scenario 
(ΔC
AB,t|PRJ
) will be estimated using a method similar to that used for the baseline. This involves 
measuring field plots alongside remote sensing techniques, supplemented by optical and RADAR 
remote  sensing  data.  By  integrating  field  data  with  remote  sensing  information, thus the 
estimation  of carbon  stock  for  each stratum can  be assessed the  changes  in  stock  between 
sampling periods. For the values applied referring to Section 3.2.1.8. 
Allometry 
The  allometry is  based  on  the  R
2
 value from  Manuri  2017  (Referring  to Table  3.1), as  this  has 
been  used to  calculate  all  forest  type  (excluding  mangrove  forest)  for  Java and Bali as  well  as 
Nusa Tenggara, Sulawesi and Maluku in the Indonesia 2
nd
 Forest Reference Level submission to 
UNFCCC (Republic of Indonesia, 2022). This allometry was applied from the baseline data using 
the same approach as employed earlier in estimating the merchantable volume.   
 
Measurements and Determining Sample Plot Carbon Stocks  
Referring to the Figure 3-1 for the methodology apply in the field measurement, the same SOP’s 
will  be used as  the field  plots  is  a Permanence Sampling Plots,  consisting  of tree  tagging  and 
geocoordinate location. Thus, any minimum values employed in inventories are held constant for 
the duration of the project. The carbon stock in the aboveground biomass of each tree species 
within  the  sample  plot  in  stratum (VF  and  LOA) will  be  estimated  using  the  chosen allometric 
equation based on the tree's dimensions. 
Determining Stratum Carbon Stocks and Determining Mean Carbon Stocks 
As the calculation of the baseline carbon stocks, the same approach will be applied in the project 
emissions to determine the stratum and mean carbon stocks. Combining the field measurement 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

114 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
with the satellite data (i.e. remote sensing), the stratum and mean carbon stock can be produced 
to assess the ongoing forest growth.

biomass is calculated as follows:

Same as the fire disturbance, it is assumed that such an event (non-fire disturbance) would have 
happened in both the project and baseline scenarios. Thus, project emissions are equal to the 
biomass  damage  from  the  non-fire  natural  disturbance  that  is not  present in  the  baseline 
scenario  (where  it  has  been  harvested  and  removed)  but  present  in  the  project  scenario.  It  is 
conservatively  assumed  that  the  natural  disturbance  is  a  stand-replacing  event,  and  that  the 
change in biomass due to the disturbance is released in the year when the disturbance occurs.  
Furthermore, it is assumed that the area disturbed is only from the illegal logging for the ex-ante 
estimation with a total of 700 ha based on the participatory rural appraisal (PRA) occurring in the 
year 2021.  
 
Figure 3-5. Identified area disturbed by illegal logging activities.  
 
For the post-ante, the disturbances and illegal logging will be derived from the PRA (every 2 years) 
and sampling as suggested by the VM0010 methodology. If the results of the PRA suggest that 
there is a potential for illegal logging activities, then limited field sampling must be undertaken 
combining with the data from Global Forest Watch GLAD deforestation alerts
42
. This secondary 
data  will guide the team to where the potential illegal  logging  may occur during  the  monitoring 
period. This will be complemented with an analysis of optical data products (Landsat & Sentinal-
2) as a remote sensing approach.

as a result of illegal logging must be calculated as:

The  VM0010  Equation  37 not  only  accounts  for  the  reduction  in  carbon  stocks  due  to  illegal 
logging  but  also  includes  emissions  from  the  fuel  used  in  these  operations  and  potential 
emissions  from  any  infrastructure  development  needed  to  support  illegal  logging.  To  estimate 
emissions  from  infrastructure  development associated with  illegal  logging, an  expansion  factor 
is  calculated  based  on  typical  baseline  harvesting  practices,  which  compares  the  volume 
extracted due to infrastructure to the merchantable volume extracted. 
For  the  ex-ante  estimation,  the net  carbon  stock  changes  as  a  result  of  illegal  logging are 
37,037.64 tCO
2
e per  annum, this  approach  is  considered  conservative. The  proposed  project 
activities are expected to completely prevent illegal logging within the project area throughout the 
project lifetime.  
3.2.2.3 Section 8.2.3 Calculation of Net greenhouse gas emissions in the project scenario 
The  net  greenhouse  gas  emissions  in  the  project  scenario  are  calculated  by  adding  the  net 
emissions from fire and non-fire forest disturbances, plus any carbon stock changes caused by 
illegal  logging,  and  then  subtracting  the  annual  carbon  stock  increase  in  the  aboveground 
biomass of trees due to forest growth. Thus, net greenhouse gas emissions in the project scenario 
in year t is calculated as:

3.2.3 Leakage Emissions (VCS 2.5, 3.2, 3.6, 3.15, 4.3) 
3.2.3.1 Activity shifting leakage 
PT Strata Pacific has control only over resource use in the project area of SERCOVA and has no 
access to other forest resource, thus the activity shifting leakage is not applicable for SERCOVA. 
There are no other forestry company owned by PT Strata Pacific. Therefore, the leakage due to 
activity  shifting is  zero,  and the  only  type  of  leakage  emissions  calculated  are  the  greenhouse 
gas emissions resulting from market effects as the implementation of project activities described 
in the section below. 
3.2.3.2 Market Leakage  
Leakage  resulting  from  market  effects  is  calculated  by  multiplying  the  net  emissions  from 
planned timber harvest activities in the baseline scenario by a suitable leakage factor, as follows: 
 
퐺퐻퐺
퐿퐾|퐿푡푃퐹,푡∗
=퐿퐹
푀퐸
∗ 퐺퐻퐺
푁퐸푇|퐵푆퐿,푡∗
         VM0010 Equation 40 
 
Where:   
GHG
LK|LtPF,t*
 : Total market leakage as a result of IFM LtPF activities, in the year t* 
since the start of the project activity (tCO
2
e) 
LF
ME
 : Leakage factor for market-effects calculations (dimentionless) 
GHG
NET|BSL,t*
 : Net greenhouse gas emissions in the baseline scenario in the year t* 
since the start of the project activity (tCO
2
e) 
 
Referring to Box 2 in the VM0010 methodology, the market leakage is determined by considering 
the country logging will be inclined of the decreased timber supply due to the implementation of 
the  project. The  extent  of  leakage  is  determined  based  on  where  harvesting  is  likely  to  be 
displaced within the country’s forest estate. If  harvesting  is  shifted  to  forests  where  a  smaller 
proportion of the biomass consists of merchantable material compared to the project area, then 
higher  emissions are  expected, as  more trees  will need to  be cut  to achieve  the  same volume. 
Conversely,  if  the  displacement  forest  has  a  higher  proportion  of  merchantable  biomass  from 
commercial  species  than  the  project  forests,  then  a  smaller  area  will  need  to  be  harvested, 
resulting in lower emissions.

Furthermore, the number of Verified Carbon Units (VCUs) for each year t in the project crediting 
period   represents   the   greenhouse   gas   emission   reductions   and   removals,   adjusted   for 
uncertainty and risk. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
126 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Adjustment for uncertainty 
Based on the VM0010 methodology, the adjustment of the uncertainty can be estimated at the 
95%  of the  confidence  interval  level  where  the  estimated  variance  is not exceeded than ±15% 
from the mean. As the best practice, it is suggested to assess the uncertainty in the early step, 
which may consist of errors in the measurement and monitoring of carbon pools and greenhouse 
gases. For both the baseline and project scenarios, the total uncertainty is determined by taking 
the square root of the sum of the squared uncertainties of each component. This calculation is 
performed  at  the  time  of  reporting  by  propagating  the  errors  in  both  the  baseline  and  project 
carbon stocks.  
Thus, total uncertainty for LtPF project is estimated as follows:

scenario (dimensionless); and

3.3.3  Monitoring Plan (VCS, 3.16, 3.20) 
As the data and parameters to be monitored in Section 3.3.2 is required to: 
a) Assess changes in forest carbon stocks and greenhouse gas emissions resulting from the 
project activities 
b) Confirm the implementation of the project activities; and 
c) Evaluate  changes  in  forest  carbon  stocks  and  greenhouse  gas  emissions  due  to 
disturbances and illegal logging. 
The monitoring plan includes the project implementation, assessing actual carbon stock changes 
from project activities and estimation of ex-post changes derived from disturbances to the forest.  
The description of the monitoring plan in the project documents will include the following for each 
of these monitoring tasks: 
a) Technical description of the monitoring task 
b) A list of data and parameters to be collected 
c) Overview of data collection procedures 
d) Quality control and quality assurance procedure 
e) Data archiving, and 
f) Organization and responsibilities of the parties involved in all the above. 
The current forest growth in the project scenario will be monitored using an approach involving 
the  re-measurement  of the  permanence  sampling  plots (refers  to Step  1:  Forest  Volume 
Inventory) on the project area in combination with remote sensing (e.g. LiDAR, Landsat, MODIS 
Terra  and  Sentinal-2  remote  sensing  data). This  combination  aims  to obtain precise data for 
producing  the  estimation  of  the carbon  stock  per  strata and  changes within  the  monitoring 
periods.  
For  monitoring  forest  disturbances  (fire,  non-fire,  including  illegal  logging), the field  team  will 
monitor the presence of any disturbances or just outside the project area and on a regular basis. 
As this project collaborates with authorities and the stake holders for the surveillance or ground 
patrolling.  The  project  activity  also implements the remote  sensing  data (LiDAR,  Landsat  & 
Sentinal-2 remote sensing data, GFW GLAD deforestation alerts) as an alert and a monitor tool 
for guiding the team to check the specific location where the potential disturbances occurred.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

Furthermore, for measuring, recording, storing, aggregating, collating and reporting on monitored

i. The organizational structure, responsibilities and competencies of the personnel that will be

The organizational structure including their responsibilities and competencies for implementing

Figure 3-7. Step by step field measurement for monitoring 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
140 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Those in charge of carbon measurement must receive comprehensive training in both field data 
collection  and  analysis. As  the  QA&QS,  developing  Standard  Operating  Procedures  (SOPs) for 
every stage of field carbon measurement is advisable. These SOPs should meticulously outline 
each phase of the measurement process to ensure reproducibility. Furthermore, a documented 
verification  of  all  quality  assurance  and  quality  control  procedures should  be  generated  and 
included in the project records. 
An audit program should be established to monitor field measurements and sampling. Typically, 
this monitoring plan involves three types of checks. 'Hot checks' involve auditors observing field 
team members  during  data  collection  at  a  specific  site,  primarily  for  training  purposes.  'Cold 
checks'  occur  independently  of  the  field crews'  presence during the audit. 'Blind checks'  entail 
auditors completely re-measuring a plot without prior knowledge of the original measurements.  
Hot  checks  enable  immediate  correction  of  technique  errors,  while  blind  checks  assist  in 
calculating  measurement  variances.  Upon  completion  of  fieldwork,  approximately  10%  of  plots 
should be checked independently. The data collected during these checks are compared with the 
original data, and any discrepancies are identified, corrected, and documented. Error rates are 
expressed as a percentage of all rechecked plots to estimate measurement errors. 
iv. Laboratory 
For  laboratory  measurements,  the  SOPs  consist  of  steps,  such  as  calibrating  instruments  to 
measure  a  total  carbon  or  specific  carbon  forms  using  certified  standards  that  are  available 
commercially. To be noted, it is essential to calibrate periodically all scales applied for measuring 
dry  weights,  following  the  valid  standard  from  their  manufacturer  with fine  scale balances. 
Reanalyzing  or  reweighing  10  to  20  percent  of  samples,  where  feasible,  assists  in  assessing 
errors. The laboratories team should ensure to record each step in order to follow the procedures. 
v. Data Entry 
Accurate  input  of  data  into  analysis  spreadsheets  is  essential  as  this  step  may  overlap  if  the 
initial  field  data  are  collected  electronically.  Continuous  communication  among  all  team 
members  who  are  responsible for  data  collection  and  analysis  is  crucial  to  address  any 
discrepancies before finalizing the analysis of monitoring data. If anomalies arise and cannot be 
resolved,  the  data  from  those  plots  should  be  excluded  from  the  analysis.  Cross-checking  the 
data input sources reduces the number of errors. 
vi. Data Archiving 
As  the  forestry  activities  are  relatively  long-term  period,  the  data  that  will  be achieved (i.e. 
maintenance and storage) should consist of: 
• The  original  copies  of  both  field  and  laboratory  measurement,  such  as  tally  sheet  and 
electronic  files  should  be  maintained  in  original  form  (i.e.  saved  in  electronic  format or 
stored in a secure location). 
• All  data  analyses,  models,  the  final  carbon  sequestration  estimate,  GIS  products,  and 
monitoring reports should be stored securely (preferably offsite). 
Therefore, if nonconformance s with a validated monitoring plan arise, the project proponent will 
be able to access and update the monitoring plan to comply with the satisfaction of the VVB and 
VCS program officers.  
vii. Policies for oversight and accountability of monitoring activities 
All  data  collected,  recorded,  stored  and  reported  are  subject  to  review  and  approval  by  team 
leaders  and/or  project  managers  with  reference  to  written  SOPs  covering  each  level  of  data 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
141 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
management.  A  copy  of  the  SOPs  is  available  to  validators  on  request.  In  order  to  ensure  the 
security  and  traceability  of  data  entry  and  QA/QC  procedures,  all data consist  of a unique 
password in order to access the database, and the access can be restricted as appropriate. 
 
3.3.4 Dissemination of Monitoring Plan and Results (VCS, 3.18; CCB, CL4.2) 
The monitoring plan and the results of monitoring conducted in accordance with this plan will be 
disseminated  and  made  publicly  available  through  multiple  channels.  Detailed  reports  will  be 
accessible  on  the  Verra  website,  ensuring  transparency  and  broad  accessibility.  Additionally, 
summaries of the monitoring plan and its results will be communicated to communities and other 
stakeholders through several means: 
• Free,  Prior,  and  Informed  Consent (FPIC)  Procedures:  Information  will  be  shared  in 
accordance with FPIC protocols to ensure that communities are fully informed and can 
provide feedback. 
• Village Administration  Offices: Posters summarizing key aspects of the  monitoring  plan 
and  results  will  be  displayed  on  the  news  boards  in  each  village  administration  office, 
ensuring local visibility. 
• Seram Green Forum: Discussions and presentations of the monitoring plan and results 
will  take  place  during  meetings  of  the  Seram Green  Forum,  fostering  dialogue  and 
engagement. 
• SERCOVA  Website:  Relevant  information  will  be  published  on  the  SERCOVA  website, 
providing a centralized source of information for interested parties. 
• Social  Media  Platforms:  Updates  and  summaries  will  be  shared  through  social  media 
channels,  including  Instagram and LinkedIn  to  reach  a  wider  audience  and  facilitate 
broader communication. 
These measures are designed to ensure that the monitoring plan and its results are accessible 
and communicated effectively to all relevant stakeholders. 
3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits  
This  project  aims  to  restore  and  protect  natural  habitats,  enhance  biodiversity,  and  support 
community  resilience  in  the  face  of  climate  change.  By  focusing  on  nurseries  for  native,  food-
trees, and Rare, Threatened, and Endangered (RTE) tree species, we aim to restore ecosystem 
functions and create sustainable habitats. The project also includes community engagement and 
training to promote climate-smart agriculture and alternative livelihoods. 
3.4.1 Key Aspects of Climate Vulnerability for Biodiversity 
1. Increased Climate Risk and Vulnerability: Biodiversity is increasingly at risk due to more frequent 
and severe weather events such as storms and floods. These events can lead to habitat destruction 
and fragmentation, making it difficult for species to survive and thrive. 
2. Phenological Changes: Changes in the timing of biological events, such as flowering and breeding, 
can disrupt food availability and breeding patterns. This can have cascading effects on ecosystems, 
affecting species that rely on specific timing for their life cycles. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
142 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
3. Extinction Risk: Species with narrow ecological niches, particularly endemic species, are at a higher 
risk of extinction. These species often have specialized habitat requirements and limited ability to 
adapt to changing conditions. 
3.4.2 Potential Impacts on Ecosystems 
1. Habitat Destruction and Fragmentation: Severe weather events can lead to the destruction of 
natural habitats, making it difficult for species to find suitable living conditions. This can 
result in reduced biodiversity and weakened ecosystem functions. 
2. Disruption of Food Chains: Changes in phenology can disrupt food chains, affecting species 
at all levels. For example, if a plant species flowers earlier than usual, the pollinators that rely 
on it may not be able to adjust their life cycles accordingly, leading to a decline in both plant 
and pollinator populations. 
3. Increased Poaching and Habitat Degradation: As habitats become more fragmented and 
resources scarcer, there may be an increase in poaching and other forms of habitat 
degradation. This further threatens the survival of vulnerable species. 
3.4.3 Adaptation Strategies 
1. Protect  Existing  Natural  Habitat:  Ensuring  the  protection  of  existing  natural  habitats  through 
regular forest patrols can help reduce habitat destruction and maintain ecosystem functions. 
2. Restore  Habitat  and  Ecosystem  Functions:  Restoration  activities,  such  as  planting  native  and 
rare,  threatened,  and  endangered  (RTE)  species,  can  help rebuild  ecosystems  and  provide 
suitable habitats for species. 
3. Ensure Nurseries and Habitat Restoration Activities: Establishing nurseries for native, food-trees, 
and RTE tree species can support habitat restoration efforts and provide food sources for various 
species. 
4. Monitor  Breeding  Patterns:  Identifying  and  monitoring  the  breeding  patterns  of  trigger  species 
and those with narrow ecological niches can help in understanding and mitigating the impacts of 
climate change on these species. 
5. Implement Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) for Disaster Response: Ensuring that SOPs for 
disaster response are in place and regularly drilled can improve preparedness and response to 
climate-related emergencies. 
6. Stakeholder  Training   and   Coordination:   Training  stakeholders   in   implementing  SOPs  and 
ensuring  effective  communication  and  coordination  with  relevant  authorities  can  enhance 
emergency response and support biodiversity conservation efforts. 
By  addressing  these  key  aspects  of  climate  vulnerability  and  implementing  targeted  adaptation 
strategies,  we  can  work  towards  protecting  biodiversity  and  maintaining  healthy  ecosystems  in  the 
face of climate change. 
3.4.4 Key Aspects of Climate Vulnerability for Communities 
Increased Climate Risk and Vulnerability 
Communities,  especially  those  reliant  on  agriculture  and  fishing,  face  heightened  risks  due  to 
climate change. These risks include more frequent and severe weather events, such as storms, 
floods, and droughts, which can disrupt local economies and livelihoods. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
143 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Impact on Biodiversity 
Climate change can lead to a loss of biodiversity, affecting ecosystems that communities depend 
on  for  food,  clean  water,  and  other  resources.  This  loss  can  exacerbate  the  vulnerability  of 
communities by reducing the availability and quality of these essential resources. 
Health Issues 
Rising  temperatures  and  changing  weather  patterns  can  lead  to  health  problems,  such  as 
heatstroke and respiratory issues, particularly among vulnerable groups like the elderly. Regular 
health services are essential to mitigate these impacts. 
3.4.5 Potential Impacts on Communities 
Disruption of Local Economies 
Communities  that rely  on agriculture and  fishing are  particularly  vulnerable  to climate change. 
Changes in temperature and precipitation patterns can affect crop yields and fish populations, 
leading to economic instability. 
Food and Water Security 
Climate change can affect the availability and quality of clean water and food resources. This can 
lead  to  food  insecurity  and  health  problems,  particularly  in  communities  that are  already 
vulnerable. 
Livelihood Challenges 
As  traditional  livelihoods  become  less  viable  due  to  climate  change,  communities  may  face 
increased poverty and unemployment. This necessitates the exploration of alternative livelihood 
options to ensure economic stability. 
3.4.6 Adaptation Strategies 
1.  Community Awareness and Training 
Training  on  Adaptation  Measures:  Providing  training  to  communities  on  various  adaptation 
measures can help them better prepare for and respond to climate risks. 
Increasing   Awareness: Raising   awareness   about   climate   risks   and   vulnerabilities   can 
empower communities to take proactive steps in mitigating these impacts. 
2.  Climate-Smart Practices 
Climate-Smart  Agriculture:  Promoting  agricultural  practices  that  are  resilient  to  climate 
change can help maintain crop yields and food security. This includes using drought-resistant 
crops, efficient water management, and sustainable farming techniques. 
Climate-Smart  Fishing:  Implementing  sustainable  fishing  practices  can  help  protect  fish 
populations and ensure long-term viability of fishing as a livelihood. 
3.  Health and Vulnerable Groups 
Regular Health Services: Facilitating regular health services and ensuring they are accessible 
during climate events can help mitigate health impacts. 
Targeting Vulnerable Groups: Identifying and regularly monitoring vulnerable groups within the 
community  ensures  that  adaptation  measures  are  inclusive  and  effective.  Developing 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
144 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) and a monitoring system can help ensure that project 
activities are targeting these groups. 
4.  Livelihood Diversification 
Alternative  Livelihoods:  Providing  alternative  livelihood  options  can  reduce  dependency  on 
climate-sensitive  sectors.  This  can  include  training  in  new  skills,  promoting  sustainable 
ecotourism, and increasing access to better markets for community products. 
Community  Employment:  Employing  local  communities  in  adaptation  projects  can  provide 
immediate economic benefits and build local capacity for long-term resilience. 
5.  Monitoring and Implementation 
SOPs and  Monitoring Systems:  Developing and  implementing SOPs  and  monitoring  systems 
ensures  that  adaptation  projects  are  effectively  targeting  and  benefiting  vulnerable  groups. 
Regular training and monitoring of project staff are crucial for successful implementation. 
By addressing these potential impacts and implementing comprehensive adaptation strategies, 
communities   can   enhance   their   resilience   to   climate   change   and   ensure   sustainable 
development. 
3.4.7 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (CCB, GL1.1) 
In  the  absence  of  the  project,  the  likely  regional  or  sub-national  climate  change  would  pose 
various impacts to the Project Zone’s ecosystem and IPLCs’ economy. 
From the ecosystem perspective, the absence of the process would drive breeding patterns due 
to changes in phenology in a long term. This will increase the extinction risk for narrow ecological 
niches  species,  particularly  endemic  species  in  unique  biogeographical  region  such  as  this 
SERCOVA Project. A total of 28 endemic species recorded in the Project Area and 15 migratory 
species inhabits Project Area and Project Zone. Hence, the regional climate change scenario will 
likely drive these impacts to biodiversity.  
From  the  IPLCs’  economy,  the  absence  of  the  process  would  disrupt  local  economies  as 
communities are heavily rely on agriculture and fishing as their main livelihood. In the absence 
of  the  project,  the  regional  climate  change  will  likely  affect  the  farming  period  and  fishing 
locations.   Prolonged   drought   and/or   rain   season   increase the   risk   and   vulnerability   for 
agriculture.  While  the  regional  climate  change  will  likely  affect  the  sea  condition,  temperature, 
and wind that also increase the risk and vulnerability for fishing.  
3.4.8 Climate Change Impacts (CCB, GL1.2) 
Impacts on Community Wellbeing 
Climate change poses significant threats to community wellbeing, affecting various aspects of 
daily life and overall health. Key impacts include: 
• Health Issues: Increased temperatures can lead to heatstroke and exacerbate respiratory 
problems, particularly among the elderly. More frequent and severe weather events, such as 
storms and floods, can cause injuries and increase the spread of waterborne diseases. 
• Food and Water Security: Changes in climate disrupt local agriculture and fishing, leading to 
reduced availability and quality of food and clean water resources. This can result in 
malnutrition and food insecurity. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
145 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Economic Disruption: Communities reliant on agriculture and fishing face economic instability 
due to unpredictable weather patterns and reduced yields. This can lead to loss of income and 
increased poverty. 
• Increased Vulnerability: Vulnerable groups within the community, such as the elderly, children, 
and those with pre-existing health conditions, are at greater risk from climate impacts. Ensuring 
these groups are identified and monitored is crucial for effective response. 
 
Impacts on Biodiversity Conservation 
Climate change also significantly affects biodiversity, with consequences for ecosystem health 
and species survival: 
• Habitat Destruction: Increased frequency of extreme weather events can lead to habitat loss 
and degradation, making it difficult for species to survive. 
• Disruption of Ecological Functions: Changes in phenology disrupt food availability and breeding 
patterns, particularly for species with narrow ecological niches or those that are endemic. 
• Increased Extinction Risk: Species that cannot adapt quickly to changing conditions face a 
higher risk of extinction. This is especially true for those with limited ranges or specific habitat 
requirements. 
 
3.4.9 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (CCB, GL1.3) 
To mitigate these impacts, several adaptation measures are necessary: 
For Community Wellbeing 
1. Health Services: Facilitate regular health services to address climate-related health issues and 
provide training on adaptation measures. 
2. Disaster Preparedness: Develop and drill Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) for disaster 
response, ensuring effective communication and coordination with relevant authorities. 
3. Alternative Livelihoods: Provide training and support for alternative livelihoods, such as climate-
smart agriculture and fishing, to reduce economic vulnerability. 
4. Community Awareness: Increase community awareness on climate risks and vulnerabilities 
through education and training programs. 
5. Market Access: Improve access to better markets for community products to enhance economic 
resilience. 
For Biodiversity Conservation 
1. Habitat Restoration: Implement nurseries for native, food-trees, and Rare, Threatened, and 
Endangered (RTE) species. Restore habitats and ecosystem functions to support biodiversity. 
2. Regular Patrols: Conduct regular forest patrols to reduce habitat destruction and poaching, 
ensuring the protection of existing natural habitats. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
146 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
3. Monitoring Systems: Identify and monitor breeding patterns of trigger species and those with 
narrow ecological niches to inform conservation strategies. 
4. Climate-Smart Practices: Promote climate-smart agriculture and fishing practices to ensure 
sustainable use of natural resources. 
5. Community Involvement: Employ local communities in conservation projects and provide 
training to increase their involvement and ownership of biodiversity conservation efforts. 
By implementing these measures, communities can enhance their resilience to climate change, 
ensuring both human wellbeing and the conservation of biodiversity. The causal model for climate 
adaptation is shown in diagram below.

4 COMMUNITY 
4.1 Without-Project Community Scenario  
4.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CCB, CM1.1) 
Administratively, the project area is in the Seram Bagian Timur Regency (SBT) and included in 5 
districts: Bula, Bula Barat, Teluk Waru, Tutuk Tolu, and Kilmury. Covering Bula and Salas in Bula 
District, Bula Barat District there are Jembatan Basah, Rukun Jaya, and Hote, Teluk Waru District 
includes Dawang, Solang, Belis, Boinfia, and Waru Villages, Tutuk Tolu District is only one village, 
namely Gah, and Kilmury District is in Selor and Kilmury Villages.  
The long history of villages in East Seram (SBT) can be divided into three main phases that reflect 
different dynamics, namely the Ternate and Tidore Sultanate phase, the Dutch Colonial phase, and 
the Indonesian Independence phase. These three phases mark major changes in the communities 
that inhabit the SBT region. The changes brought about in these phases can be seen from several 
historical  relics  around  SBT  villages.    Before  the  influence  of  the  Ternate  and  Tidore  Sultanate, 
community  groups  practiced  a  nomadic  lifestyle  by  living  in  mountainous  areas.  They  lived  by 
hunting and gathering patterns whose relics can be seen from sacred locations in caves and forests 
around the village.  
The arrival of royal influence made community groups begin to migrate to the beaches, the arrival 
of the Dutch in Bula with the discovery of oil mining locations made the coast a new area that was 
more advanced as a trading area. And in Indonesian Independence phase, transmigration villages 
were formed from 1998 - 2001, namely Jembatan Basah and Rukun Jaya Villages. The following 
villages are classified as transmigrant and non-transmigrant or local villages (Table 4-1) 
Table 4-1. Villages around the project areas distributed by village history

4.1.1.1 Well-Being Information 
Livelihood 
Communities  in  the  study  villages  depend  on  a  variety  of  livelihoods.  Agriculture  and  fisheries 
form the backbone of the local economy, with sago and fish being the main commodities. Sago, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
148 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
as  a  staple  food,  is  not  only  consumed  in  the  form  of  traditional  food  but  also  processed  into 
other  products  such as  sago  flour and sago  plates.  In addition  to  sago, nutmeg, cloves, cacao, 
and seasonal fruit crops are commodities in plantations. Furthermore, some villages rely on crops 
that can be harvested more than once a year such as vegetables, bananas, and secondary crops. 
Fisheries, both in fresh and marine waters, provide a source of protein for the population as well 
as  a  source  of  income  through  the  sale  of  catches. Each  village  usually  has  similar  livelihood 
categories, although some are different, such as in transmigration villages and district capitals 
like  Bula.  The  main  livelihoods  are  less  dependent  on  forests,  gardens,  and  agriculture,  Bula 
focuses more on monthly work, trading, and fishing.

Income 
Income data is presented as average monthly income in 13 villages around the project area. The 
average income data serves as a crucial tool in assessing the feasibility of this region for the carbon 
project,  particularly  in  identifying  impoverished  areas  within  the  project  scope  to  serve  as  a 
foundation  for  economic  assistance  programs.  By  examining  the  income  levels  across  villages, 
project  planners  can  pinpoint  areas  with  lower  average  incomes,  indicating  potential  economic 
vulnerabilities  and  areas  in  need  of  support.  This  information  becomes  instrumental  in  tailoring 
economic aid programs to address the specific needs of these communities, thereby maximizing 
the impact of the carbon project beyond environmental conservation. Understanding the economic 
landscape  through  income  data  facilitates  the  identification  of  targeted  interventions  aimed  at 
uplifting disadvantaged populations and fostering sustainable development in the region. 
The  East  Seram  Regency  in  Maluku  Province  faces  significant  economic  challenges,  with  most 
villages  having  an  average  monthly  income  below  the  national  poverty  line  of  IDR  2,592,657. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
149 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Villages  such  as  Kilmury,  Selor,  Gah,  Dawang,  Solang,  Boinfia,  and  Bellis  have  monthly  incomes 
ranging  from  IDR  1,250,000  to  IDR  1,875,000,  well  below  the  poverty  line.  Even  villages  with 
slightly  higher  incomes,  such  as  Salas  (IDR  2,075,000),  Hote  (IDR  2,020,000),  and  Jembatan 
Basah   (IDR   1,990,000),   remain   below   the   poverty   threshold,   reflecting   difficult   economic 
conditions for many residents.

diversification in the village, which also has a much larger population compared to other villages. 
With a  population  of  11,614, Bula likely has  better  infrastructure and wider access to  economic 
resources.

Health 
The  table  below  details  the  number  of  healthcare  facilities  and  personnel  in  the  project  area. 
Healthcare facilities include public health centers (Puskesmas) or auxiliary health centers (Pustu) 
and  integrated  service  posts  (Posyandu).  An  integrated  service  post  is  a  healthcare  community 
basis run by volunteers called village health workers or cadres. The services of integrated service 
posts include mother and child health monitoring, socialization of birth control, child immunization, 
and other simple community health monitoring. Each village has at least one healthcare facility and 
each village’s health facilities are supported by healthcare professionals. The nearest hospitals are 
in Bula, the hospitals are called RSUD (Rumah Sakit Umum Daerah).

4.1.1.2 Community Characteristics 
Ethnicity Practices and Values 
Traditional  values  are  not  lost  even  though  the  majority  of  the  community  has  embraced  Islam. 
When  opening  a  new plantation, there are still  rituals that  must  be carried out,  for example, the 
sina ritual by the Bati people, which is intended to ask permission from the ancestors as the owner 
of the forest location that is being cleared to become a plantation. In the ritual, people give offerings 
in  the  form  of  betel  nut,  cigarettes  and  several  types  of  food  such  as  rice  and  sago  and  other 
accompanying  foods.  Offerings  are  given  accompanied  by  burning  incense  and  reading  prayers. 
Firbulik is a tradition that the community still preserves today. Every man who wants to get married 
will invite all adult men in his neighborhood to discuss the good intentions of the prospective groom 
to participate both in terms of funds and labor. This culture is still maintained in Negeri Solang and 
Dawang. 
Not much different from Cakalele, Tikam Dabus is also a typical Seramese art that is also part of 
the culture of the Salas / Dawang community. In its implementation, this culture is mostly carried 
out  during  holidays  or  traditional  ceremonies,  such  as  Eid  al-Adha,  and  the  custom  of  delivering 
bodies (kings, priests, community/customary leaders). During Eid al-Adha, the sacrificial animals 
will be paraded around the village along with adult men stabbing their chests with an iron rod with 
a pointed tip. From this action, blood will flow from their chests which is inteIreted by the community 
as  a  form  of  sacrifice  for  their  love  for  the  Prophet  Ismail  AS  who  was  almost  slaughtered.  In 
organizing the event, all villagers work together from the preparation process until the event is over. 
The implementation process is led by certain people who have the highest knowledge and authority 
in the tikam dabus tradition, such as the Imam of the Mosque.  
People  who  want  to  participate  in  the tikam  dabus tradition  are  required  to  perform  ablutions 
before carrying out this tradition with the aim that they are protected from unwanted things. The 
provision of stabbing tools to the community is also not arbitrarily given, but the priest will give it 
with  the  reading  of  prayers.  This  tradition  is  usually  carried  out  by 6-8  people  who  can  be  done 
alternately by jumping up and down by stabbing tools into their chests. The tifa and tambourine are 
used as musical accompaniment in this event.  
History and Mythology  
Seram Island, especially in the Eastern Seram region, is rich in mythology that has been passed 
down from generation to generation. These mythological stories cover various aspects of people's 
lives,  from  the  influence  of  the  Ternate-Tidore  kingdom  to  folklore  about  forests  and  ancestors. 
These mythologies serve not only as entertainment or bedtime stories, but also as a guide to life 
that  governs  the  customs,  beliefs  and  rituals  that  are  still  practiced  today.  The  mythology  of  the 
Ternate-Tidore  kingdom:  During  the  reign  of  Sultan  Nuku,  Ternate-Tidore  began  to  expand  its 
territory  to  Seram  Island,  especially  East  Seram  and  Gorom  Island.    As  explained  by  the  Tidore-
language  bebeto  manuscript  found  in  Waru  Bay,  the  manuscript  is  a  story  of  the  journey  of  the 
Tidore sultanate's Islamic broadcasting in the Teluk Waru area. 
• Folklore of the Forest and Ancestors: The Kilmury community has a story in the forest. In the 
past to go to the Waru area, the ancestors walked through the forest, to fulfill the needs of 
provisions.  While walking  they  planted  fruits  and  tubers  in  the forest.  So,  until  now  when 
walking in the forest, the Kilmury community is not afraid of hunger because there is food 
that was planted by their ancestors.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
152 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• An interesting mythology in Belis and Kilmury is that if a story/history is told it will bring death 
to the storyteller, and according to him everyone who has a story is sworn if he is not allowed 
to tell just any story, he must be in a certain condition and there are prerequisites that must 
be fulfilled such as betel nuts. 
• In  addition,  there  are  several  myths  regarding  marriage,  in  a  particular  clan,  for  example 
people from the Melantutu clan are prohibited from marrying people from outside, especially 
migrants  who  are  not  from  Seram  Island.  Except  through  a  ritual  first  by  visiting  a  sacred 
tomb near the water reservoir belonging to Negeri Belis. In this ritual, the bride must bring 
betel nut, machete, and several other things. At the tomb, the bride and groom accompanied 
by the matarumah ask permission and inform them that someone is coming and will marry 
their children and grandchildren. The ritual must be carried out otherwise it will bring disaster 
not only to the bride and groom, but also to the father-in-law who comes from the Melantutu 
clan. Mr. Hasan Fesanlau, who is the head of Saniri Negeri, said that in the 2000s there was 
a  person  from  the  Melantutu  clan  married  to  a  newcomer,  the  bridegroom  who  was  a 
newcomer went around the village, while walking around he passed nutmeg trees, soon all 
the nutmeg trees he passed died, nothing was left. 
Government Structure and Customs  
Village  governance  in  Maluku  Province  encompasses  not  only  modern  governmental  structures 
regulated by laws but also traditional elements that play a crucial role in maintaining social stability, 
local wisdom, and cultural sustainability. Among the figures playing significant roles in the village 
governance structure are the Latupati, Raja Negeri, and Saniri. Each of these figures has unique 
roles, duties, and authorities in fostering community harmony and preserving traditional customs. 
The following is the structure of customary institutions in Maluku Province: 
 
Figure 4-2. Customary institution structure in Maluku (Source: Research Analysis Social Study, SBT 
2024 
The Latupati, as the customary leader, plays a significant role in maintaining traditions and customs 
in  his  area.  His  main  task  is  to  maintain  harmony  and  harmony  among  the  villagers  in  his 
community.  In performing his  duties,  the Latupati is  often  the  mediator  in  resolving  conflicts 
between villagers. Latupati divided into two levels: Latupati at the province level and Latupati at 
the district level. His authority is based on local customs and traditions, allowing him to decide on 
customary  matters  that unresolved  at  the  village  level,  provide  advice  to  the  village  head,  and 
officiate at important district or provincial ceremonies. The Latupati is not only a leader, but also a 
custodian of local wisdom values passed down from generation to generation. 
Latupati
(Maluku Province)
Raja Negeri
Raja Negeri
Raja Negeri
Latupati 
(District) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
153 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Raja Negeri serves as the head of traditional governance with the highest authority in their area. 
Their primary role is the administration of traditional governance and maintaining social stability 
and security within their territory. The Raja Negeri is also responsible for preserving local wisdom 
and  regional  culture.  Their  authority  includes  making  decisions  related  to  local  governance  and 
social life, convening traditional meetings, and facilitating local development. The presence of the 
Raja Negeri is not only as an authoritative figure but also as a preserver of the local wisdom values 
integral to the identity of the Maluku community. 
Lastly, the Saniri is a respected figure chosen by the community to represent their interests at the 
local government level. Their main role is as a mediator between the community and the village or 
provincial government. The Saniri also plays a crucial role in advancing the aspirations and needs 
of the community to the authorities. Their authority involves providing input and advice to the village 
government and conveying the community's aspirations to higher authorities. The presence of the 
Saniri underscores the importance of community participation in decision-making processes and 
local development. Overall, the roles, duties, and authorities of the Latupati, Raja Negeri, and Saniri 
in  village  governance  in  Maluku  Province  reflect  the  close  relationship  between  modern  policies 
and  traditional  customs.  Although  village  governance  has  undergone  transformation  with  the 
adoption  of  more  formal governance  systems,  the  presence  of  these  traditional  figures  remains 
crucial  in  preserving  local  wisdom  and  maintaining  social  stability.  Their  presence  is  not  only  as 
leaders or authorities but also as guardians of cultural values and traditions that define the Maluku 
community.

The data table above is a snapshot of the population in the villages surrounding the project area, 
which  depicts  a  landscape  rich  in  demographic  variation. First, the villages  cover  a  spectrum  of 
population sizes. Some villages, such as Boinfia and Solang, are small in scale with populations of 
241 and 428 respectively, while others, such as Kilmury, have larger populations, totaling 1,102. 
This difference in size could be due to a variety of factors, including historical settlement patterns, 
economic opportunities and geographic location. 
In  addition,  analyzing  the  gender  distribution  in  each  village  will  reveal  interesting  insights.  In 
villages  such  as  Selor  and  Bellis,  the  ratio  of  males  to  females  appears  relatively  balanced, 
suggesting a fair representation of both sexes within the community. In contrast, in villages such 
as  Bula,  where  the  population  has  soared  to  11,614,  the  sex  ratio  may  show  more  pronounced 
variations, potentially reflecting migration patterns, cultural norms, or economic activities that are 
more characteristic of urban life in Bula Village, the central location of East Seram Regency. 
Ethnicity and Religion 
Ethically most of the population in Seram Bagian Timur are Seramese, an ethnicity rich in history 
and  culture.  They  are  divided  into  two  main  groups:  Seram  Gunung  and  Seram  Pantai.  Seram 
Gunung are those who originally lived in the mountains before migrating to the coast, while Seram 
Pantai are the descendants of those who have settled in the coastal area. However, the presence 
of Orang Bati, a subgroup of Seram Gunung, adds complexity to the ethnic diversity of the region. 
In addition, the presence of migrants from other tribes such as Javanese, Bugis and Banda, who 
settled in several villages, shows that SBT is a place that is open to various cultures. 
In terms of the religious composition of East Seram, most of the population is Muslim, with a small 
number of Christians. Although most of the indigenous population has embraced Islam since the 
1998  ethnic  conflict  in  Maluku  Province,  there  is  still  a  small  number  of  migrants  who  embrace 
Christianity. This shows that religion is not only a spiritual aspect, but also rooted in the identity and 
daily lives of the people of SBT. Religious differences also reflect the complex social and historical 
diversity of the region.

4.1.2 Interactions between Communities and Community Groups (VCS, 3.19; CCB, 
CM1.1) 
Social groups formed in the project site are based on ethnicity, religion, and livelihoods. The main 
dominance of social groups is more visible based on ethnicity, with the dominant ethnicity being 
Seram ethnicity (Seram Gunung and Seram Pantai) as the earliest inhabitants of Seram Island. The 
dominant immigrant groups are from Java, Bugis and Buton. They started coming after Indonesian 
independence  through  transmigration  programs  and  trade  relations.  Relations  between  ethnic 
groups  in  the  project  area  are  not  too  different  because  of  the  similarity  of  Islam,  this  religious 
equation is the basis for harmonious relations between ethnic groups and avoids the risk of inter-
ethnic conflict. 
Religious groups in SBT are fully dominated by Islam after the religious conflict in the 2000s. Before 
the  conflict,  Christian  groups  dominated  in  several  villages  such  as  Boinfia,  Solang,  Salas  and 
Dawang, while in Bula village the Christian group reached 50%. During the conflict, many Christians 
migrated to Papua and West Seram. Many others converted to Islam such as Boinfia, Solang, Salas, 
and  Dawang.  Now,  Islam  in  the  project  area  reaches  99.9%,  which  reduces  the  potential  for 
religious conflict in the project area.  
Groups based on  livelihoods  are dominated by  farmers,  fishermen,  and traders.  Ethnic  migrants 
such  as  Javanese,  Bugis,  and  Buton  dominate  jobs  in  the  trade  sector,  while  Seramese  ethnic 
dominate jobs in the agriculture and fishing sectors. The difference in livelihoods between ethnic 
migrants and ethnic Seramese creates a mutually beneficial economic relationship. 
4.1.3 High Conservation Values (CCB, CM1.2) 
The  project  aims  to  protect  and  improve  the  quality  of  high  conservation  values  (HCVs)  that 
supported  community  well -being  in  the  project  zone.  The  project  identified  areas  that  provide 
critical ecosystem service (HCV 4), areas that are fundamental for the livelihoods of communities 
(HCV 5), and critical areas for the traditional cultural identity of communities or sacred areas for 
communities (HCV 6). This site for HCV identities based on the HCV assessment and consultations 
with local stakeholders.  
4.1.3.1 HCV 4: Ecosystem Service 
The project team identifies areas ghat provide basic ecosystem services in critical situations. The 
detailed description is provided below:  
High Conservation Value HCV 4: Within the project area connected to the villages there are 
rivers,  wells,  and  springs  which  are  their  water  source  for daily 
needs and garden needs (for transmigrant villages)

Qualifying Attribute 
Protection the rivers, wells, and springs areas:  
Rivers in the project zone are Garikit Rivers, Salas Rivers, Waifufa 
Rivers, Wailala Rivers, Bolivar Rivers, and Masiwang Rivers all the 
rivers  are  still  used  for  daily  needs  of  water  by  community  in  the 
project  zone.  The  upstream  river  needs protection to  protect  the 
infiltration  areas.  The  upstream  river  has  an  important  ecological 
function  for  water  availability  in  the  project zone.  The  wells  and 
several  springs  in  the  concession  area  are  used  as  clean  water 
reserves during long dry seasons.

4.1.3.2 HCV 5: Basic Needs of Community 
Humans in living their lives need various types of goods and services to meet their needs. Some 
of these needs are basic needs and some are complementary. Areas that have an important 
function as a source of livelihood for local communities. Especially in meeting basic needs, are 
areas that have HCV 5.  
High Conservation Value HCV 5: Forest 
Qualifying Attribute 
HCV  5  examines  the  relationship  between  humans  and  forests  to 
meet their daily needs. Some areas in the forest are also old villages, 
formerly shifting cultivation areas where their ancestors used to grow

of  the  people  in  community  still  need  non-timber  forest  products, 
wood  forest  products,  and  deer  or  birds  hunting.  Identified  HCV  5,

4.1.3.3 HCV 6: Traditional Cultural of Community 
High Conservation Value Old village in forests, hills, springs and rivers. 
Qualifying Attribute HCV 6 is very important for communities because it relates to areas 
that  have  important  functions  for  cultural  identity,  beliefs  or  other 
traditional benefits for local communities. Through HCV 6 protection, 
communities  can  maintain  their  cultural  heritage,  which  includes 
sacred  sites,  ritual  sites  and  other  important  areas  that  are  an 
integral part of community identity and well-being. Additionally, this 
protection  helps  ensure  that  valuable  traditional  practices  and 
knowledge can be preserved and passed on to future generations. In 
this  way,  HCV  6  contributes  to  the  survival  of  local  culture  and 
wisdom, as well as supporting social and ecological sustainability. 
Focal Area 672.63 Ha 
  
 
Figure 4-5. Map of HCV 6 
4.1.4 Without-Project Scenario: Community (CCB, CM1.3) 
The  initial  condition  of  the  community  without  the  project  scenario  is  the  economic  activities  of 
agriculture, fisheries, and forestry.  
a. Economy: The main livelihood in the community is in the agricultural sector with the main 
commodities  of  nutmeg,  cloves,  seasonal  fruits,  and  secondary  crops.  Other  livelihoods 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
159 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
that are quite common in the community are fishermen, traders, hunters, timber seekers, 
NTFP seekers, and agarwood seekers. The average income from these occupations in all 
villages is Rp1,736,538.46 per month.  
b. Education:  The  participation  rate  in  tertiary  education  is  low  due  to  remote  access  and 
expensive  tuition  fees. There  is  only  one  private  higher  education  facility  in  Kabupaten 
Seram  Bagian  Timur.  The  participation  rate  in  junior  secondary  and  senior  secondary 
schools is quite high, although facilities and infrastructure in schools are still lacking. 
c. Health:  Access to  health  facilities  is  quite  difficult  in  some  villages  such  as  Kilmury  and 
Selor because there are no doctors, so health services are only available at the puskesmas 
level. Health services at the Regional General Hospital in Bula are also unable to handle 
diseases that require more sophisticated medical equipment, and the community must go 
to Ambon City for this.  
d. Energy: Access to electricity in two villages: Kilmury and Selor is still not available.

training, and seed assistance.

farmers' gardens as has happened in several villages.

welfare  of  farming  families.  Fire  prevention  will  prevent  farmers 
from huge losses in crop losses. It is expected that in the first 10 
years, 50% of wellbeing indicators will be increased.

4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.2) 
1 Hunter: Deer and bird hunters, with the project hunting scenario there will be restrictions and 
reduced  income.  As  a  risk mitigation  action,  socialization  about  the  project  area  is  needed, 
because the project area is only a small part of the hunting area, and people can hunt outside 
the project area. The community will also be invited to become company employees who can 
provide additional income instead.  
2 Illegal Loggers: There are still many locations to look for wood outside the project area. 
3 Farmer: There  are  several  villages  whose  farm  locations  are  included  in  the  project area; to 
mitigate negative risks the company will make the location a social management project area. 
The social management area will be implemented by planting fruit trees and forestry plants that 
are expected to increase community income. 
4 Local Community: The project area is included in the community's customary area, even some 
important sites such as old villages, heirloom storage, and other cultural areas are included in 
the project area. It is likely that this will be a potential conflict; to mitigate this risk the project 
scenario  will  be  designed  to  recognize  and  protect  customary  territories  and  important 
community sites.

4.4 Community Impact Monitoring  
4.4.1 Community Monitoring Plan (CCB, CM4.1, CM4.2, GL1.4, GL2.2, GL2.3, GL2.5) 
Project activities have identified communities and community groups that are directly or indirectly 
affected. List of stakeholders can be seen in appendix 1 and details in section 2.3.1. Monitoring 
will be carried out on variables that have been included in the Theory of Changes, some of these 
variables are economic income, access to higher education, and access to health. Measurements 
will be taken before the project and during the project. The measurement period is carried out 
twice  a  year,  to  see  the  changes  that  occur  in  each  semester.  At  the  beginning  of  the  project, 
changes  may  not  be  visible  because  there  are  several  activities  that  have  not  yet  been 
implemented.  
Monitoring will be carried out through three activities, as follows: 
1. Qualitative method through Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA). 
2. Quantitative method through survey with sampling method aimed at affected groups and 
non-affected groups.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
166 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
3. Measurement  can  also  be  used  using  national  standard  measurements  recognized  by 
the government through government agency data.  
4.4.2 Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, CM4.3) 
The community  monitoring  plan  will  be  disseminated  to the community,  local government, and 
other stakeholders around the project area. Important documents related to the project will be 
provided to the village, sub-district, and district governments. Dissemination of monitoring plan 
information will also be organized through village meetings and village notice boards. Monitoring 
results will also be disseminated through the project website that can be seen by all stakeholders 
and communities. 
4.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Community Benefits  
4.5.1 Exceptional Community Criteria (CCB, GL2.1) 
Under the VCS+CCB Standards, there are also criteria that acknowledge the social co-benefit and 
increase  the  project  integrity.  These  criteria known  as the  Exceptional  Community  Benefits.  The 
project meets the CCB criteria for Exceptional Community Benefits as the majority local population 
is below the national poverty line. According to the data, 12 out of 13 villages have average incomes 
below the national poverty line, with average monthly incomes ranging from IDR 1,250,000 to IDR 
2,075,000. Only Bula has an average income above the national poverty line. 
4.5.2 Short-term and Long-term Community Benefits (CCB, GL2.2) 
In the short term, this project is expected to provide benefit for community on: 
• Community  will  be  provided  employment  opportunities  (involved  in  project  activities) 
through the company’s worker recruitment program through the village government.  
• Community  will be improved access to health  services  through  the  village  health  service 
improvement program.  
• Community  will have improved  access  to  higher  education  (for  their  children)  through 
scholarship assistance programs and cooperation with universities.  
• Community  will  be  increased  income  through  agricultural  development  programs  and 
financial assistance programs for Micro, Small, and Medium Enterprise (UMKM).  
In a long term this project is expected to provide benefit for community on: 
• Improving  the  quality  of  life  of  the  community  through  empowerment  programs  in 
education, health, and economy. 
• Forest  conservation:  carbon projects often  focus  on  preserving  existing  forest  and 
reforesting  degraded  areas.  This  helps  in  maintaining  biodiversity,  protecting  water 
sources, and preserving ecosystems that are vital for local communities.  
• Protection of Forest as Cultural Heritage: most villages in the project area has a significant 
cultural and spiritual value to the forest. Carbon programs that support forest conservation 
also  contribute  to  the  preservation  of  important  cultural  and  spiritual  sites.  This  is 
important for maintaining their cultural identity and heritage. 
4.5.3 Community Participation Risks (CCB, GL2.3) 
The  relationship  between  local  villages  and  PT  Strata  Pacific  shows  significant  variation.  The 
villages  experiencing  conflicts  with  PT  Strata  Pacific  are  Gah,  Boinfia,  and  Bellis.  These  conflicts 
are  primarily  due  to  issues  of  land  ownership and  resource  utilization.  In  contrast,  the  village  of 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
167 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Waru  supports  PT  Strata  Pacific,  likely  because  of  the  company's  involvement  in  community 
development  programs  that  benefit  the  villagers.  Other  villages,  such  as  Kilmury,  Selor,  Salas, 
Dawang, Solang, Hote, Jembatan Basah, Rukun Jaya, and Bula, display a neutral attitude towards 
PT  Strata  Pacific.  This  neutral  stance  may  be  due  to  a  lack  of  direct  contact  or  limited 
communication  with  the  company.  This  indicates  opportunities  for  PT  Strata  Pacific  to  enhance 
communication and collaboration with these villages. 
For a carbon project of PT Strata Pacific with communities around the project area, implementing 
Free,  Prior,  and  Informed  Consent  (FPIC)  is  crucial  for  ensuring  communities  have  a  meaningful 
role in  decisions  affecting  them.  FPIC  process  that  emphasized  transparency,  openness  and 
community participation can prevent negativity in decision making and community involvement in 
the project. Village meetings and public consultation will be held as a medium for socialization and 
evaluation of the project with the community following the IFC Performance Standard (2012). This 
project will also create, train, and evaluate Feedback, Grievance, Redressing Mechanism (FGRM) 
to  ensure  the  project  equality,  just,  transparency  and  continues  improvement  for  community 
participations.

4.5.5 Net Impacts on Women (CCB, GL2.5) 
One of the project's aims is to enhance women's roles within the community. To accomplish this, 
several activities will be specifically tailored for women's groups: 
• An alternative livelihood program offering training and financial support for the business 
development of women's groups. 
• A  training  program  focused  on  involving  women  in  decision-making,  emphasizing  the 
importance  of  including  women's  perspectives  in  decisions  that  traditionally  overlook 
their issues. 
• Ensuring  women's  participation  in  all village meetings, including FPIC meetings and 
Village Forums. 
These activities are intended to boost the involvement of women in village affairs. Social studies 
at the village level indicate that women's participation in decision-making remains low, and issues 
like domestic violence are not adequately addressed in village discussions. Despite this, women 
play a significant role in household income through activities such as trade, and agriculture. 
4.5.6 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (CCB, GL2.6) 
In  the  early  stages  of  the  project  through  the  FPIC  process,  the  project  engaged  with  local 
communities  to  discuss  the potential  benefits,  costs,  and  impacts  of  the  project.  This  process 
included   identifying   the   communities'   expectations   regarding   the   project's   benefits.   All 
community groups participated in the decision-making process, as detailed in Appendix 5: FPIC 
Consultation  Report.  Agreements  were  made  on  activities  to  be  carried  out  in  each  village  to 
ensure local communities benefit from the project. 
Moving  forward,  the  project  will  maintain  transparency  in  discussing  benefit  sharing  with  local 
communities and will plan and implement project activities in a participatory manner. The agreed-
upon  use  and  distribution  of  benefits  will  be  managed  through  village  meetings  involving  all 
community  groups  to  guarantee  transparency  and  fairness.  This  process  will  be  overseen and 
monitored by the project team and representatives of the local communities. 
In  the  early  stages  of  the  project,  the  project  location  was  identified  as  overlapping  with  the 
village's  plantation  and  settlement  areas.  The  project  planning  changed  to  remove  the  project 
areas  that  fell  within  the  plantation  and  settlement  areas  so  there  was  no  project  impact  on 
community property rights. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
4.5.7 Benefits, Costs, and Risks Communication (VCS, 3.18; CCB, GL2.7) 
In the early stages of the project, an explanation of benefits, costs and risks was provided to the 
local government, sub-districts, villages and communities through socialization and the provision 
of project-related documents.  
To ensure that the information is understood by local government and community members, 
several methods were used, as follows: 
• Ensure  that  all  group  representatives  in  the  community  participate  in  the  FPIC  process, 
ensuring  that  all  group  representatives  understand  all  benefit,  cost  and  risk  information 
provided.  
• Ensure that all group representatives sign the FPIC.  
• Provide supporting project documents to village government representatives. 
• Provide  additional  information  in  the  form  of  leaflets  distributed  in  the  villages  and 
participate in village activities and convey explanations about the project. 
4.5.8 Governance and Implementation Structures (CCB, GL2.8) 
The  implementation  of  the  project  from  the  beginning  involved  the  local  government,  sub-
districts, villages, and related institutions. This level of government was involved from the project 
planning  through  the  FPIC  process  which  involved  them  in  project  planning,  project-related 
documents  were  also  provided  at  the  planning  stage  to  ensure  that  the  information  provided 
could be understood transparently and clearly.  
At  the  community  level, involvement  is  also  carried  out  from  the  beginning  of  project  planning 
through the FPIC stage by providing comprehensive information about the project and providing 
opportunities  for  the  community  to  be  involved  in  determining  project  plans.  Each  village 
representative and representatives of groups in the village are involved in determining the project 
development  plan around the community area.  Input  and recommendations provided are used 
as project planning materials and some are included in project implementation activities such as 
agricultural, education and health development programs in the village. 
 
4.5.9 Smallholders/Community Members Capacity Development (CCB, GL2.9) 
In the project design, there are several activities that directly or indirectly contribute to developing 
the  capacity  of  individuals  and  youth  groups  in  the  village.  These  activities  are  recruitment  of 
workers from the village, forest patrols, ecotourism assistance and research assistance. As the 
project is designed to involve the community in project implementation, the provision of training 
will add capacity and knowledge as well as awareness of the importance of the project site.  
Workers from the village will be given training according to the position and competence of the 
job, so that they can develop a career in the magamenent project or can work in other companies 
with  higher  positions  and  competencies.  Forest  patrol  invites  forest  user  groups  to  be  actively 
involved and get benefit income from forest patrol work, training is provided to improve individual 
skills  in  forest  management  and  forest  patrol.  Ecotourism  and  research  assistance  will  invite 
youth groups and forest users who previously did a lot of hunting and logging in the forest to get 
involved in bird and animal watching ecotourism. They will receive training on knowledge of the 
flora  and  fauna  of  the  project  site,  and  this  knowledge  will  further  enhance  their  capacity  to 
become  research  assistants  in  collaboration  with  research  institutions  and  universities  from 
within and outside the country.

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario  
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B1.1) 
PT Strata Pacific is located on the East side of Seram Island. The project location is a part of the 
global terrestrial ecoregion of “the Seram rainforests area” (Olson, 2024). The majority of its area 
is forested, with primary and secondary categories. The current primary forest area is 54,140 ha, 
and the current secondary forest area is 2,910 ha.  
The  total  large  of  PT  Strata  Pacific  is  ±73,365  hectares  at  upstream  river  area.  Besides  the 
important area of biodiversity, the project area also provides ecosystem services to the downstream 
region as a natural flood prevention area. In the west side of the project area located the “Gunung 
Tuntuan Protected Forest”. The project area is a natural buffer area for protected forest and the 
biodiversity insides. 
Total biodiversity found in the Project Zone is 289 spesies, consisting of 133 fauna species and 
156  tree  species  were  found.  Most  of  the  species  recorded  within  the  project  area  are  forest-
dependent species. Some of them are sensitive to disturbance (e.g., selective logging). Biodiversity 
species from groups such as hornbills, cuscus, and cockatoos are dependent on trees, if the canopy 
of trees in the habitat declines it will affect the population of them. Under the BAU, the habitat will 
be highly disturbed, and the biodiversity population will decline.  The selective logging activities had 
been done in the early activities of PT Strata Pacific between 2016-2022. 
Species and 
habitat  
Total  biodiversity  found in  the  Project  Zone  is  289 species,  consisting  of  133 
fauna  species  and  156  tree  species  were  found. The  structure  of  the  fauna 
composition   found   consists   of   18   mammal   species,   80   bird   species,   8 
amphibian species, and 27 reptile species.  The species found represent of the 
fauna species commonly found in Seram Island or in Maluku Region. But these 
findings represent only a small fraction of the total animals present on the entire 
Maluku Region and the Wallacea Region as a whole. Mammal species account 
for 34%,  avifauna  for  43%,  herpetofauna  for  72%  of  Seram  Island's  total 
(Macdonald, Hill,  Boeadi,  &  Cox, 1993;  Eaton, van  Balen,  Brickle,  &  Rheindt, 
2022; Edgar & Lilley , 1993). 
The habitat types in the project area are mature secondary dryland forest, young 
secondary   dryland   forest,   shrubland,   and   community   plantation.   Project 
activities will not adversely affect the species or their habitats as the project will 
implement  Improve  Forest  Management  (IFM)  by  converting  logging  activities 
into forest protection activities. 
Areas 
needed for 
habitat 
connectivity 
The project area is not a corridor connecting one forest area to another forest 
area but the project area is a buffer for the Gunung Tuntuan Forest Protected 
(HL Gunung Tuntuan) and the intact forest landscape.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
172 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Threats to 
Biodiversity 
The threats to biodiversity in the project area are twofold: declines in species 
populations and declines in the size and quality of natural habitats. The 
population decline is caused by poaching. Poaching in the project area occurs 
due to the demand for illegal wildlife trade, and individual demand for wildlife 
as tokens (i.e., trophy, pets). The decline of natural habitats is caused by illegal 
logging, uncontrolled fire, and uncontrolled natural disturbance. 
 
 
Figure 5-1. The project area is adjacent to protected forest (Hutan Lindung) Gunung Tuntuan 
 
5.1.2 High Conservation Values (CCB, B1.2) 
In the project area, we found a total of 67 species (60 fauna and 7 flora) were identified as High 
Conservation Value (HCV) species (Table 5-1 
),  particularly  HCV  1.  HCV  1  is  fauna  or  biodiversity  species  that  are included  in  the  Vulnerable, 
Endangered, and Critically Endangered categories according to the IUCN Red List, Appendix I and 
II of the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES), 
protected by the Government of Indonesia (MoEF Regulation No. P106/2018), migration species, 
and has a limited distribution, in this case limited to Seram  Island and small islands in  around 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
173 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Seram (Wallacea Area). Based on the IUCN Red List, there are 8 fauna species listed in the VU, EN 
and CR categories. Currently, mammals and flora are the most listed with 3 species each. Based 
on CITES, 23 species are included in Appendix I and II categories. From the list of protected species 
by the Ministry of Environment and Forestry, there are 27 species. 
The endemic category has the highest total species in HCV 1 category. Namely, species that are 
included in the limited category. Seram Island is the part of the Wallacea Region. Since it was first 
described by Alfred Russel Wallace in 1,869, Wallacea Region it has had very different biodiversity. 
The  animals  on  Wallacea  Region  are  like  a  transition  between  animals  in  the  areas  of  western 
Indonesia (orientalis) and eastern Indonesia (australis) . All area in PT Strata Pacific is included in

Certain area of the project zone is included in the intact forest landscape. It makes the area are 
included in the HCV 2 category. An intact forest landscape (IFL) is a seamless mosaic of forest 
and  naturally  treeless  ecosystems  with  no  remotely  detected  signs  of  human  activity  and a 
minimum area of 500 km
2
. IFLs are critical for stabilizing terrestrial carbon storage, harbouring 
biodiversity,  regulating  hydrological  regimes,  and  providing  other  ecosystem  functions.  The 
reduction of IFL from 2000-2013 in Indonesia was 10.8% (Potapov, et al., 2017). 
The project zone is included in the endemic bird area and the key biodiversity area. An Endemic 
Bird  Area  (EBA)  is  defined  as  an  area  which  encompasses  the  overlapping  breeding  ranges  of 
restricted-range species, such that the complete ranges of two or more restricted-range species 
are entirely included within the boundary of the EBA 
44
. All project area is included in the Endemic 
Bird  Area,  meanwhile  the  Key  Biodiversity  Area  is  only  in  the  northern  part  of  the project  area.  
Key  Biodiversity  Areas  (KBAs)  are  the  most  important  places  in  the  world  for  species  and  their 
habitats.  Faced  with  a  global  environmental  crisis  we  need  to  focus  our  collective  efforts  on 
conserving  the  places  that  matter  most.  The  KBA  Programme  supports  the  identification, 
mapping, monitoring and conservation of KBAs to help safeguard the most critical sites for nature 
on  our  planet – from  rainforests  to  reefs,  mountains  to  marshes,  deserts  to  grasslands  and  to 
the deepest parts of the oceans
45
.
